Victoria Church of England Infant and Nursery School -

Similar schools comparison report

Monitor your school attendance

Autumn term 2025 to 2026 (September to mid-December)

Data protection and data sharing

This report contains sensitive information about your school.

You must store and share the data securely. Read the data protection impact assessment (DPIA) (PDF, 241 KB, 21 pages) for the daily attendance data collection.

Version history

This table shows changes to your summary report for the current academic year.

What is the similar schools comparison report?

This report is a summary of your attendance for pupils in years 1 to 6 compared to 20 similar schools. It’s tailored specifically to your school.

You’ll receive a new report at the end of each half and full term. This helps you build on your progress throughout the year.

We use artificial intelligence, machine learning and expert judgement to highlight where your school is performing well and where there may be opportunities for improvement. This is based on comparisons to similar schools within your geographical region.

Similarities are based on 7 key factors that impact school attendance. These factors include the proportion of pupils eligible for free school meals (FSM) and those with social, emotional and mental health (SEMH) needs.

You can find full guidance on this report in the user guide, and learn how we calculate and group your similar schools in the methodology paper, available in View your education data (requires login). For some schools, there are small differences between the data in this report and data contained in the Monitor your school attendance service. The user guide includes information about why these differences occur.

Attendance baseline improvement expectation (ABIE)

From the start of the 2026 to 2027 academic year, mainstream schools will receive an ABIE. This is the minimum improvement expected for your school this academic year. Many schools will target and deliver improvements well above their ABIE.

The purpose of the ABIE is to lift the floor on attendance improvement. Schools who meet or exceed it help to sustain the strong rates of attendance improvement made in the 2024 to 2025 academic year.

At the end of the academic year, we’ll compare your school’s attendance outcomes against your ABIE to understand progress and impact.

Your report last half-term included your indicative ABIE for the 2025 to 2026 academic year. Your indicative ABIE has not changed. You should continue to use your attendance improvement ladder to benchmark your current performance against your ABIE, local and national averages, performance of similar schools and pre-pandemic levels of attendance.

What this means for your school

Schools that significantly exceed their ABIE may be contacted by the Department to share their good practice across the sector.

If your school does not meet its ABIE for more than one year or misses it substantially, your school may be offered further support to help get back on track.

Not meeting your ABIE does not lead to formal intervention and Ofsted does not have access to your ABIE.

Named similar schools

This report includes names and Unique Reference Numbers (URN) of the similar schools within your group with higher outcomes for overall and persistent absence. You can search Get information about schools by URN to identify named schools.

You can use this information to contact these schools and compare attendance strategies.

The final page of this report explains how to contact similar schools with higher attendance.

Your indicative ABIE

Your school’s indicative ABIE means your attendance needs to be 0.4 percentage points higher for the 2025 to 2026 academic year than for the 2024 to 2025 academic year, as measured by this report.

This is the minimum improvement you should expect to make this academic year.

If your school meets or exceeds this ABIE, your pupils will gain at least 58 extra school days, compared to last year.

Your ABIE is a starting point, not a limit. We know many schools will target and achieve gains well beyond their ABIE. We encourage you to aim high.

Your school’s attendance for the first half-term may be above your indicative ABIE. ABIEs are minimum expectations for the whole academic year. Attendance typically declines as the year progresses, so early gains are essential to staying on track.

Your school attendance improvement ladder

Your school attendance improvement ladder shows how your school can move beyond your baseline, drawing on your pre-pandemic attendance and the strongest-performing similar schools as useful reference points. It suggests a pathway towards pre-pandemic levels or higher. Each increase brings greater positive impact for your pupils.

Your similar schools comparison

Areas of relative strength

Attendance on Tuesday mornings was 97.5%, higher than 18 out of 20 similar schools.

Year 1 attendance was 97.4%, more than 16 out of 19 similar schools. You also had fewer persistently absent pupils in year 1 than 13 out of 19 similar schools. Some comparisons with similar schools were not possible because that school did not have this year group, or only reported data for a small number of pupils in this year.

Attendance on Wednesday afternoons was 97.3%, higher than 15 out of 20 similar schools.

Areas to focus on

We have identified 3 areas where similar schools have achieved a higher attendance.

If you can match their achievements in these three areas, then your overall attendance rate would increase from 96.3% to 97.9%. This means the proportion of your pupils who are persistently absent would drop from 8% to 8%.

Year 2 attendance was 95.6%, lower than at least a quarter of similar schools. If you increased it to 96.7%, then your overall attendance would go up by 0.6% percentage points. Your overall persistent absence would stay the same.

Attendance of pupils with special educational needs (SEN) support was 92.4%, lower than at least a quarter of similar schools. If you increased it to 95.6%, overall attendance would increase by 0.6%. Your overall persistent absence would stay the same.

Attendance on Fridays was 95.2%, lower than at least a quarter of similar schools. If you increased it to 96.6%, overall attendance would increase by 0.3%%. Your overall persistent absence would stay the same.

Headline figures compared to similar schools

This section shows overall attendance and persistent absence figures for your pupils in years 1 to 6 compared to 20 similar schools.

We calculate results based on data from the start of the 2025 to 2026 academic year up to Monday 22 December 2025.

Overall attendance

Your overall attendance is 96.3% which is 0.2% higher than the median attendance of similar schools. You rank 8th of 21 schools for attendance. This means 13 schools have lower overall attendance than yours.

The 4 schools with the highest overall attendance are:

Goldfield Infants’ and Nursery School (URN 117274)

Harvey Road Primary School (URN 117131)

Kingshill Infant School (URN 117319)

The Russell School (URN 117098)

Persistent absence

Your persistent absence is 8% which is 0.6% lower than the median attendance of similar schools. You rank 9th of 21 schools for persistent absence. This means 12 similar schools have a higher rate of persistent absence than your school.

The 4 schools with the lowest persistent absence are:

Broxbourne CofE Primary School (URN 117437)

Goldfield Infants’ and Nursery School (URN 117274)

Harvey Road Primary School (URN 117131)

The Russell School (URN 117098)

You can search Get information about schools using these URNs to find contact details.

Sources: Monitor your school attendance results for your school and similar schools, data from the DfE.

Notes:

Attendance and absence figures for your school and individual pupils are updated daily via the Monitor your school attendance data dashboard.

Contacting similar schools with higher attendance

Contacting similar schools with higher attendance may provide you with further sources of support. You should read the DfE guidance on improving attendance before contacting other schools. Guidance includes:

Working together to improve school attendance - provides statutory guidance for schools, trusts and local authorities, and outlines effective strategies for improving attendance.

Summary table of responsibilities for school attendance - gives a concise overview of attendance roles and responsibilities across the system.

Attendance Toolkit - shares approaches, case studies and resources from system leaders to help schools improve attendance.

Toolkit for schools: communicating with families to support attendance - offers practical examples and insights to help schools talk with families about attendance.

Finding contact details

You can find contact details for similar schools with higher attendance within your group using:

Get information about schools - search by school name or URN to find names of school leaders, associated trusts, and links to school websites.

School websites - most schools publish contact details and leadership team information. Use enquiry forms or the contact details of attendance leads where available.

When contacting schools, you may wish to ask them about:

Data and targeted support: how to gather, share and use attendance data to put in place action to improve attendance.

Culture: how to embed a support-first culture to encourage high attendance and make school a place children want to attend.

People: how to make attendance ‘everyone’s business’ and ensure individuals and teams work together to reduce absence.

Processes and systems: how to develop and implement strong and rigorous processes to identify and respond quickly and effectively to absence.

Relationships: how to build strong and positive relationships with pupils and families to understand, then prevent or remove barriers to attendance.

Communications: how to develop and share strong and inclusive attendance communications that convey messages with impact.

Transition: how to ensure new pupils joining your school feel they belong at school and are clear on expectations around attendance.

The school does not give the weakest readers the support they need. Pupils read books that do not match precisely enough the sounds they know. This means that pupils do not learn quickly to read with fluency. The school needs to review the support given to early readers, and ensure that this is provided effectively, so that the weakest readers remember their sounds better and can use these to build up their skills and confidence in reading. | The school does not give the weakest readers the support they need. The school does not use assessment as effectively as it could to check and ensure that pupils remember what they have learned in the longer term. As a result, some pupils lack the sticky knowledge they need to build up a detailed understanding of their subjects. The school needs to ensure it uses the information from checks on learning, both in lessons and at the end of units of learning, to adapt the next curriculum delivery effectively This will enable pupils to remember more of what they learn in the longer term. | The school does not use assessment as effectively as it could to check and ensure that pupils remember what they have learned in the longer term. Context | Context | Context | Context | Context | Context | Context

School Context Overview 
Whole School Context
Victoria C of E Infant and Nursery School is a small infant school within the Castle Federation, working closely with Thomas Coram to ensure curriculum consistency, shared values and high expectations for all pupils across the federation. The school serves Nursery, Reception, Year 1 and Year 2 pupils. Leadership is strong, with the Head of School appointed in September 2022 and the Executive Headteacher in January 2024. The school has a strong safeguarding culture, effective governance and a clear vision centered on inclusion, equity and strong foundations for learning. Outcomes in phonics, EYFS and KS1 are consistently above national and Hertfordshire averages, with reading a clear strength following rapid and decisive improvement actions.
The school community includes pupils with a wide range of needs, including SEND, pupils eligible for Pupil Premium and children with emerging speech, language and communication needs. Attendance and behaviour are strong, supported by clear systems, rigorous monitoring and close partnership with families. Staff professional development is prioritised to ensure consistent, high-quality provision.
Class-Level Context
Classes are inclusive, adaptive and focuses on quality first teaching, with teaching structured around a clearly sequenced and well-designed curriculum. Teachers use regular formative assessment to identify gaps in learning and adapt teaching accordingly. Reading, oracy and foundational skills in English and maths are prioritised in all classes. Adults are deployed strategically to support learning, particularly for vulnerable pupils, while maintaining high expectations for all. Classroom environments are calm, purposeful and adapted to support regulation, independence and engagement.
Group Context (e.g. SEND, Pupil Premium, EAL, Vulnerable Pupils)
The school adopts an equity-based approach to meeting the needs of groups of pupils. Pupils with SEND and those eligible for Pupil Premium are identified early and supported through high-quality teaching, targeted interventions and personalised adaptations. The HFL Every Child (VIP) programme ensures that pupils with potential barriers to learning have a named champion, with progress, attendance and wellbeing closely tracked. Small-group and ‘keep up’ interventions, particularly in phonics and early reading, are used to prevent pupils from falling behind. Attendance and engagement of vulnerable groups are monitored rigorously, with strong parental partnership and external agency support where needed.
Individual Pupil Context
Every pupil is known well by staff. Individual strengths, needs and barriers to learning are identified through baseline assessments, ongoing formative assessment and regular pupil progress meetings. Support plans, adaptations and interventions are tailored to the individual and reviewed regularly for impact. For pupils with complex additional needs, early identification, personalised provision and close collaboration with parents and external professionals ensure timely and effective support. Pupil voice is valued, and children are supported to develop confidence, resilience and a strong sense of belonging within the school community.
This contextual overview underpins teaching, assessment, inclusion and pastoral support across the school, ensuring that all pupils are supported to flourish academically, socially and emotionally.
Two teachers currently on maternity leave.
One more teacher going on maternity leave start of Feb 2026
HLTA is currently on maternity leave. | School Context Overview 
Whole School Context
Victoria C of E Infant and Nursery School is a small infant school within the Castle Federation, working closely with Thomas Coram to ensure curriculum consistency, shared values and high expectations for all pupils across the federation. The school serves Nursery, Reception, Year 1 and Year 2 pupils. Leadership is strong, with the Head of School appointed in September 2022 and the Executive Headteacher in January 2024. HLTA is currently on maternity leave. | School Context Overview 
Whole School Context
Victoria C of E Infant and Nursery School is a small infant school within the Castle Federation, working closely with Thomas Coram to ensure curriculum consistency, shared values and high expectations for all pupils across the federation. The school serves Nursery, Reception, Year 1 and Year 2 pupils. Leadership is strong, with the Head of School appointed in September 2022 and the Executive Headteacher in January 2024. The school has a strong safeguarding culture, effective governance and a clear vision centered on inclusion, equity and strong foundations for learning. Outcomes in phonics, EYFS and KS1 are consistently above national and Hertfordshire averages, with reading a clear strength following rapid and decisive improvement actions.
The school community includes pupils with a wide range of needs, including SEND, pupils eligible for Pupil Premium and children with emerging speech, language and communication needs. Attendance and behaviour are strong, supported by clear systems, rigorous monitoring and close partnership with families. Staff professional development is prioritised to ensure consistent, high-quality provision.
Class-Level Context
Classes are inclusive, adaptive and focuses on quality first teaching, with teaching structured around a clearly sequenced and well-designed curriculum. Teachers use regular formative assessment to identify gaps in learning and adapt teaching accordingly. Reading, oracy and foundational skills in English and maths are prioritised in all classes. Adults are deployed strategically to support learning, particularly for vulnerable pupils, while maintaining high expectations for all. Classroom environments are calm, purposeful and adapted to support regulation, independence and engagement.
Group Context (e.g. SEND, Pupil Premium, EAL, Vulnerable Pupils)
The school adopts an equity-based approach to meeting the needs of groups of pupils. Pupils with SEND and those eligible for Pupil Premium are identified early and supported through high-quality teaching, targeted interventions and personalised adaptations. The HFL Every Child (VIP) programme ensures that pupils with potential barriers to learning have a named champion, with progress, attendance and wellbeing closely tracked. Small-group and ‘keep up’ interventions, particularly in phonics and early reading, are used to prevent pupils from falling behind. Attendance and engagement of vulnerable groups are monitored rigorously, with strong parental partnership and external agency support where needed.
Individual Pupil Context
Every pupil is known well by staff. Individual strengths, needs and barriers to learning are identified through baseline assessments, ongoing formative assessment and regular pupil progress meetings. Support plans, adaptations and interventions are tailored to the individual and reviewed regularly for impact. For pupils with complex additional needs, early identification, personalised provision and close collaboration with parents and external professionals ensure timely and effective support. Pupil voice is valued, and children are supported to develop confidence, resilience and a strong sense of belonging within the school community.
This contextual overview underpins teaching, assessment, inclusion and pastoral support across the school, ensuring that all pupils are supported to flourish academically, socially and emotionally.

Strengths 
Culture of safeguarding reiterates “it could happen here” 
Staff all use CPOMS consistently and regularly to record concerns  
Positive relationships between pupils, parents and staff mean there is culture of trust and support 
Outcomes of pupil surveys- 93% felt safe at school and 100% knew who to go to if someone being bullied 
Annual Safeguarding audit with pupils- looking at safe/unsafe spaces around school, keeping safe at school etc 
Working in partnership with parents/families when making referrals to family support and improve outcomes for those families 
Termly safeguarding audit with Safeguarding governor using HFL template and reported to FGB- including audit of CPOMS  
Regular training and updates on Safeguarding 
Safety initiatives with children-e.g. road safety, NSPCC (stay safe), water safety (lifeboats), Safer internet Day, Safety online and off 
Safer recruitment processes followed, and staff trained to conduct interviews 
Low level concerns for staff recorded on StaffSafe and staff aware of procedures to report concerns 
Senior SLT across Federation support each other where there are complex cases (children/parents/staff) 
Effective partnerships with external agencies / DSPL 8/Family support  
SLT completed Prevent (train the trainer) CPD to run Prevent training to all staff
Next steps 
Maintain the vigilant culture in the school 
Induction of new staff  
Update staff on new DDSLs/ DSLs across the Federation | Strengths

Inclusion
Self evaluation: expected standard/strong standard
“Pupils with special educational needs and/or disabilities (SEND) get the help they require. Their needs are accurately identified. Staff carefully adapt learning so that pupils with SEND access the full curriculum.” Ofsted 2023

“The school demonstrates strong inclusive practice and high standards. Leaders have a clear understanding of pupil needs and implement robust SEND processes, including early identification and tailored interventions. The school uses evidence-based strategies to close attainment gaps, prioritises communication and language development, and embeds quality first teaching effectively.  Staff show commitment to parental engagement and pupil wellbeing, while attendance systems and safeguarding are rigorous. Inclusive classroom environments, differentiated teaching, and respectful pupil interactions reflect the school’s ethos of “flourishing in learning and love.” 
SEA Inclusion visit Nov 2025 | Inclusion

SEA Inclusion visit Nov 2025

HfL Every child programme - VIP program – every pupil with a potential barrier to learning has a champion and progress and attendance is being tracked. Training for all staff delivered and more scheduled focussing on classroom adaptations. High expectations and enhanced opportunities for all pupils so barriers are swifty and effectively addressed.
Early identification and intervention to address needs – starting from transition conversations 
Pupil Progress meeting to discuss every pupil / gaps / needs and interventions and adaptation 
Little Wandle's tracking and SEND interventions ensures that gaps in learning are addressed swifty  
Personalised provision for SEND/FSM ensures an equity approach to curriculum access  
Adaptations through quality first teaching using Herts Ordinary Provision toolkit and SEND toolkit to ensure all pupils can access challenge and learning is not capped
Inclusion CPD for staff on co-regulation, creating equity in learning/behaviour strategies, cognitive load, executive function and specific SEND (ADHD/ASD)
SEND early identification/referrals enables adaptations to be in place upon entry, including liaison with previous settings / visits to aid transition e.g. organising an AV1 robot for child with medical needs, working collaboratively with setting on EHCPs
EYFS Transition Toolkit completed and used to bespoke support for every child e.g. social story to use over holidays
Partnership with parents and external agencies to support pupils, tenacious approach where necessary . July 25 parent SEND survey and took views into account, lots of positive comments. 
Focus on attendance of the vulnerable pupils is forensic and interventions and partnerships with parents has had a positive impact for some of our most vulnerable pupils  
Succession planning for SENDCo  
Regular minoring and liaison with AP pupil in relation to safeguarding policies, attendance and progress   
Office booking parent evening slots for vulnerable pupils if not booked 
Parents can book an appointment with the SENCO at parent consultations
VIP confidential email address for parents to share personal issues, concerns and required support confidentially  
Learning environments are neutral to ensure pupils are not overstimulated  
The Castle Way consistencies ensure effective transition across the Federation and within each class 
Pupil Premium Strategy created using the EEF recommendations  
Progress for SEND/FSM in Little Wandle  
Y2 100% passed PSC, all SEND pupils  
Priorities
VIP programme implementation 
Tracking small steps progress evidence for those not ARE 
Create evidence of qualitative and quantitative data where gaps are narrowed for individual pupils
Sutton Report – Double Disadvantage report used to inform practice for the most vulnerable (Oct 25) | HfL Every child programme - VIP program – every pupil with a potential barrier to learning has a champion and progress and attendance is being tracked. Training for all staff delivered and more scheduled focussing on classroom adaptations. High expectations and enhanced opportunities for all pupils so barriers are swifty and effectively addressed.
Early identification and intervention to address needs – starting from transition conversations 
Pupil Progress meeting to discuss every pupil / gaps / needs and interventions and adaptation 
Little Wandle's tracking and SEND interventions ensures that gaps in learning are addressed swifty  
Personalised provision for SEND/FSM ensures an equity approach to curriculum access  
Adaptations through quality first teaching using Herts Ordinary Provision toolkit and SEND toolkit to ensure all pupils can access challenge and learning is not capped
Inclusion CPD for staff on co-regulation, creating equity in learning/behaviour strategies, cognitive load, executive function and specific SEND (ADHD/ASD)
SEND early identification/referrals enables adaptations to be in place upon entry, including liaison with previous settings / visits to aid transition e.g. organising an AV1 robot for child with medical needs, working collaboratively with setting on EHCPs
EYFS Transition Toolkit completed and used to bespoke support for every child e.g. social story to use over holidays
Partnership with parents and external agencies to support pupils, tenacious approach where necessary . July 25 parent SEND survey and took views into account, lots of positive comments. 
Focus on attendance of the vulnerable pupils is forensic and interventions and partnerships with parents has had a positive impact for some of our most vulnerable pupils

Curriculum and teaching
Self-evaluation: expected standard

‘The curriculum is well designed. It identifies what should be taught in different subjects, and when. Teachers have suitable subject knowledge. They explain new content helpfully.’ Ofsted 2023 | Curriculum and teaching

Strengths
Our pupils achieve well. Outcomes at the end of EYFS, KS1, and in the Year 1 phonics screening have been consistently in line with or above Hertfordshire and national averages
Prioritising and securing strong foundations for all pupils across the school 
Keep up not catch up – phonics is an example of this 
The school has an equity approach towards vulnerable learners to bespoke support and address barriers to their learning as part of Every Child Programme 
Reading and promoting the love of reading is strong with daily opportunities to read/be read to for pleasure ( Victoria CE Infant and Nursery School - English - Reading ) 
Oracy fluency is a focus in the core subjects (SIP) and using good practice from EYFS
Reading fluency in all year groups is embedded with children confidently reading decodable books matched to their phonic ability. Reading is a priority to ensure foundational knowledge of decoding and fluency is practiced regularly.
School focus areas target the strong foundations of communication and language, accurate and fluent reading, compositional skills, accurate spelling, handwriting and mathematical foundations 
Adults are deployed to meet the needs of pupils, particularly the most vulnerable
Annual report to governors

School Improvement Plan 

2025–2026

Flourishing in learning and love.

‘Do everything in love’ (1 Corinthians 16:14)

Priority 1: Foundation Assessments 

Children to be able to effectively retain and retrieve curriculum knowledge 

Activate prior learning and addressing misconceptions and gaps 

Priority 2:  Oracy 

Priority 3: Writing Fluency

The Writing Framework (July 2025) and Simple View of Writing

To close the writing gap by focusing on the key barriers to writing fluency:

Handwriting

Spelling

Transcription Skills

Make sure the curriculum gives enough emphasis on foundational knowledge and skills such as handwriting and writing simple sentences.

Priority 1: | Foundation Subject Assessments-used effectively to ensure children can retain and retrieve core knowledge

Priority 2: | Oracy- across the curriculum

Priority 3: | Writing Fluency- strong foundations for early writing

Objective | Action Required | Term | PR | Resources | Success Criteria

Teachers are effectively assessing the learning | All staff to understand the purpose of assessments, the appropriate time to use them 
Professional development for staff
Teachers to use responsive teaching to adapt what happens in the classroom to meet learners needs
Staff to ensure there are retrieval prompts for children to retain prior knowledge
Teachers refer to knowledge organisers at the start of each lesson to recap and at the end to consolidate learning
Teachers to be using effective questioning throughout the lessons | Aut 1

Aut 1


All

All 

All

All | HoS | HfL assessment lead | Teachers to be following the agreed assessment consistencies in the Castle Way in line with appraisal targets
Monitoring activities used to identify:
 the impact of teacher assessments 
Feedback from pupils regarding impact

Children to have a clear understanding of subject specific vocab – tier 2 / tier 3 | Every unit to have clear identified vocab
Vocab to be a key feature of knowledge organisers and to be on them
Knowledge organisers to be stuck in at the start of each topic 
Working walls to include key vocab consistently in all classrooms
Use of high-quality texts to demonstrate vocab in each subject | All | Subject Leaders 
Class teacher 
SLT | LTPlans
Vocab | Subject long term plans to make the key vocabulary explicit in each unit  
Where knowledge organisers are used in subjects, key vocabulary is explicit
Monitoring activities indicate that key vocab is consistently displayed on all curriculum working walls 
Pupil voice to identify understanding of vocab

To ensure staff understand the important concepts in their subject and unit | Using the assessment proforma create a document which outlines the topic, most important learning and assessment strategies for every unit. Subject leads to share this with staff 
Teachers to know the prior learning- where have they met this concept before?
Understand the content (what you want pupils to know/remember), concepts (what are the themes) and disciplinary learning (how does the expert think/do)
Teachers to plan lessons to support deeper learning

Autumn 25 | SLT 






Exc SLT RM | Audit

To create a robust staff training program | Introduce the idea of oracy to all staff

Helping staff understand that oracy is not just for English lessons but part of learning across the curriculum | Autumn 25 | LH/RM/LF | TLA sessions
£400 | HFL TLA to have delivered whole federation training 
Teachers' confidence in delivering strategies to develop oracy have increased
Visits to lessons demonstrate that oracy development techniques are being effectively used to engage all pupils

Developing and tracking the children’s confidence with oracy throughout the year | Baseline children’s confidence with oracy at the start of the year 
Assess at the end of the year to measure progress | Autumn | Class teachers | Assessments to include: rate your confidence levels at how confident you are in: 
Talking to a learning partner 
Talking to your friends 
Talking to adults in school 
Talking in front of your class 
Talking in class assembly

Create an oracy rich environment | Whole school display to be changed half termly demonstrating good examples of classroom activities
Classrooms to display stem sentences and staff to use them with the children in lessons | Ongoing | Class teachers | Oracy is evident around the school e.g central display, stem sentences in classrooms
During monitoring, children are using stem sentences within the classroom

Create symbols to remind children of the expectations of speaking and listening skills | Agree on symbols for the expectations of speaking and listening 
Display the symbols in classes and around the school 
Children to be reminded of these before speaking and listening activities | Sp 26 | Monitoring activities show that symbols are consistently used across the school.
Visits to classes evidence expectations for speaking and listening is happening in all lessons

To ensure that all children make progress in their oracy development | Adaptive strategies applied to regular, supported opportunities to talk for different purposes – to explore, justify, explain, reflect
Model talk as adults – in how we listen, ask questions and speak to one another
Celebrate spoken contributions with the same value placed on written work
Work with Inclusion leads to identify strategies to support children with SEN

Pupils to be involved in the development of oracy curriculum and be able to articulate the impact | All pupils to understand what oracy is 
Oracy ambassadors to be appointed in each class 
To meet half termly to share good practice | Children can articulate what oracy is and can explain it when asked 
Oracy ambassadors can share good practice and then take ideas back to the classroom to share with their own class

Handwriting Fluency- to ensure pupils can handwrite legibly and fluently. | Strengthen teachers’ subject knowledge of handwriting through CPD
Provide targeted support for children to improve handwriting/fine motor skills
Implement targeted intervention for identified focus children
Follow Good Practice for Handwriting document- using prepare (p checks) and success (s factor).
Create a handwriting lesson proforma for teachers
Purchase resources for gross and fine motor skills development
Monitor quality of handwriting teaching and learning
Conduct a baseline of transcription skills at start of Autumn term for all KS1
Introduce to children self-regulated handwriting- e.g. circle your best word/letter to set a ‘gold standard’ for handwriting. | Ongoing

Autumn 1 | LF | Teach Handwriting
NHA guidance
Fine motor skills resources- e.g. play dough and small parts | 90% of children use correct grip and letter formation by end of YR1
Increase in writing stamina- in extended writing
Consistent approach to the delivery of handwriting from N to YR2
All adults provide good models of handwriting aligned to school policy
Handwriting sessions will be set up correctly to best support the children e.g. holding pencil correctly, positioning of paper, sitting position
Cognitive demands of handwriting become less- seen in fluency of handwriting

Spelling | Develop a clearly defined (systematic approach) spelling curriculum to progress from Nursery to YR2
Embed the Little Wandle Spelling strategies in YR2 (spelling rules and common exception words)
Ensure scaffolds such as key vocabulary, common words and GPCs are available for every lesson when children write
Teach spelling through transcription to ensure this skill is automatic (free working memory)
Spelling in R and YR1 taught through phonics (LW phonic program)-to practice and apply 
Regular and consistent spelling practice- through learning new words, practice (dictated sentences), feedback, learning CEW (identify tricky part)
Develop a consistent approach to marking and assessing spelling
Clear expectations for spelling in other lessons- allow pupils to write freely and make a note of common errors to address in spelling lessons (especially words previously taught).
Create effective procedures to identify pupils having difficulty with spelling and the support to be put in place | Ongoing

Ongoing | SLT

All | Little Wandle
Spelling mats
GPC mats | 85% of children secure Grapheme Phoneme Correspondance (GPC)- improving spelling in context
Spelling accuracy evident in writing across the curriculum, especially Common Exception Words
Systematic approach to teaching spelling using Little Wandle 
Children taught how to apply their knowledge of GPCs to spell using dictation
Termly assessment will show children are on track spelling new and previously taught words
Monitoring of spelling enables early identification of children not on track and regular tasks set to practice/apply
Cognitive demands of spelling become less and children more accurate spelling in writing

Sentence construction | Promote use of varied scaffolding techniques to support oral sentence rehearsal such as speaking frames, counters and talking tins
Use strategies to support sentence construction and punctuation- e.g. sentence strips with conjunctions, rainbow sentences and walky talky sentences
CPD for teachers on strategies for supporting sentence construction and punctuation using Essentials writing
Scaffolding the writing process by checking pupils background knowledge and introduce key vocabulary
Allocate time in lessons to have an editing stop to stop, reflect and share with a peer their composition
Use editing to fix mistakes and revising to change the quality of writing- check, change, explain | Ongoing

Feedback is regular, usually oral, specific, practical, actionable and encouraging- children respond close to point of feedback to improve written work

At least 70% KS1 meet ARE in writing

[SOURCE: summary document 4.docx | Ofsted 26 Victoria /KEY DOCS | Modified: 2026-01-13]

The Castle Federation 5-Year Strategic Plan

2024-2028

What are the federation’s vision and values? 

Our Federation vision: 

“Flourishing in learning and love.”

‘Do everything in love.’  (1 Corinthians 16:14)

We have four core values at the heart of all we do. These values have been generated through our stakeholder consultation and apply equally to the whole community of schools in the Federation. The four values are: 

Kindness 

Respect 

Resilience  

Thankfulness 

What is the context for this plan? 

As part of our stakeholder consultation, Jan-July 2024, we asked all our parents, staff, pupils, governors and key members of our school community, what we did well and what we could do better. This provided us with valuable information regarding the advantages and challenges of living in our community and the challenges we feel our children face. 

Young people starting work in the next decade will work longer, change jobs more often and compete against a highly qualified international market. As well as the appropriate qualifications to compete successfully in an increasingly uncertain jobs market, young people need flexibility, creativity and transferable lifelong learning skills. 

The children leaving Year 6 this year will be young adults in 2031 and the world will look very different by then. We have used the OECD report entitled “The future of Education and Skills 2030’ to identify how our strategic plan can prepare our children for jobs that have not yet been created, for technologies that have not yet been invented, to solve problems that have not yet been anticipated. The report identifies the following key points: 

As computer technologies have displaced labour in routine tasks, they have also created new employment opportunities for workers with non-routine cognitive skills, such as creativity, and social and emotional skills. 

To remain competitive, workers will need to acquire new skills continually, which requires flexibility, a positive attitude towards lifelong learning and curiosity. 

Social and emotional skills can be equally – and in some cases even more – as important as cognitive skills in becoming a responsible citizen. 

Social and emotional skills, such as empathy, self-awareness, respect for others and the ability to communicate, are becoming essential as classrooms and workplaces become more ethnically, culturally and linguistically diverse.  

Achievement at school also depends on a number of social and emotional skills, such as perseverance, efficacy, responsibility, curiosity and emotional stability. Physical and practical skills are not only associated with daily manual tasks, such as feeding and clothing oneself, but also with the arts.  

Our strategic plan aims to enable children to be well-prepared for the future and to give them the skills they will need to thrive as adults.  

Our Strategic Plan Priorities  

Educational excellence

Safeguarding - to create a culture of safeguarding where rigorous vigilance underpins everything we do

Pupil outcomes – progress and attainment, all pupils working at or above age related expectations 

Curriculum 

Progressive and sequential 

Curriculum enrichment through trips, experiences and extra-curricular activities accessible for all 

Accessibility for all through highly effective adaptation 

Equity, diversity and inclusion 

Career programme 

Religious education and collective worship – as Church schools these elements are fundamental

Monitoring and accountability – leaders monitor the quality of teaching and implementation of the curriculum where all staff are supported to develop professionally.  

Happiness

Mental health agenda – mental health leads to ensure that this agenda is always at the forefront of support and 

Pupil well-being – ongoing and annual pupil voice 

Staff well-being – staff voice, appraisals, well-being charter 

Values and vision in action underpin everything 

Pupil responsibilities and character development 

Community

 Relationships with the local Churches, the Church of England and the Diocese 

Promotion of activities and raise awareness of issues the local, national and global communities face

Cultural capital - skills, knowledge and experiences that individuals acquire through their cultural background and upbringing  

Relationships with parents - work in partnership/ effective communication 

Local schools - work with the local HT consortium, HT at Swing Gate / Ashlyns 

Sustainability 

Finance – to ensure highly effective management of the finances to maximise pupil’s educational experiences within a framework of prudence and future planning. Taking into account external and internal environment factors. Maximise capacity for generating income. 

Staff recruitment and retention – high quality CPD, supportive appraisal processes, an environment where staff can flourish and work collaboratively

Premises – the buildings are safe, well, maintained, support learning and promote an attractive learning environment

Digital strategy – to provide a technically advanced learning environment with very strong infrastructure that is progressive, prepares pupils for the next stage of their education. Software and hardware that is renewed on a rolling programme so staff can work efficiently, and pupils’ access a high-quality curriculum. 

Environmental sustainability – pupils to be aware of the challenges faced on the planet and be proactive in addressing how we can help, locally and globally. 

Our vision and values are central to our strategic plan and underpin all aspects of school life.

Pupil premium strategy statement
This statement details our school’s use of pupil premium funding to help improve the
attainment of our disadvantaged pupils.
It outlines our pupil premium strategy, how we intend to spend the funding in this
academic year and the effect that last year’s spending of pupil premium had within our
school.

Part A: Pupil premium strategy plan
Statement of intent
At the heart of our pupil premium strategy lies a powerful belief: every child possesses
a diamond of potential, waiting to shine. We are resolute in our mission to ensure that
no child’s brilliance is dulled by disadvantage. It is our duty—and our privilege—to
uncover, nurture, and amplify that potential so every learner thrives both in school and
in life. Staff identify every pupil that has a vulnerability/possible barrier to learning,
allocating them an adult champion, as part of the local authority, Every Child Program.
We are not just investing funding; we are investing hope, ambition, and relentless
determination. Through strategic and purposeful use of our pupil premium grant, we will
ignite opportunity, dismantle barriers, and elevate outcomes for all, especially those
who need us most, ‘you let them off, you let them down’.
Our strategy is built on these objectives for transformation. We strategically plan
spending of the PPG to meet these objectives by allocating funds to:
• Further develop high quality first teaching, assessment and effective learning
strategies using research-based evidence which responds to the needs of the
individual
• Support social, emotional and behavioural needs, giving children the confidence
and resilience to support independent learning
• To improve communication, attendance and parental partnership, as this
consistently increases academic outcomes.
• Intervene swiftly, with a targeted approach at the point a specific need is identified
• Adopt a whole school approach in which all staff take responsibility for vulnerable
pupils’ outcomes and raise expectations of what they can achieve
• To create a sense of belonging where all children flourish, are valued, feel safe,
supported and included
We will champion an equity approach for our most vulnerable children where we
celebrate excellence, and never stop striving until every child, regardless of background,
is given the chance to succeed.
Challenges
This details the key challenges to achievement that we are determined to overcome. We
are unwavering in our commitment to ensuring that every child—regardless of
background—can thrive and flourish. Our assessments, observations, and reflections
have helped us identify four critical challenges that we are determined to tackle head-on:
Challenge Detail of challenge
number
1 Attendance and engagement in learning: Learning can only happen when children
are present and engaged. We are concerned that some pupils are missing too much
school or are not fully engaged when they are here. We will work closely with families
2

Challenge | Detail of challenge

number

1 | Attendance and engagement in learning: Learning can only happen when children
are present and engaged. We are concerned that some pupils are missing too much
school or are not fully engaged when they are here. We will work closely with families

and staff to improve attendance and foster a culture of curiosity, motivation, and active
learning.
2 To develop a sense of belonging where every child can flourish, achieve and
thrive: School to strengthen opportunities to develop background knowledge and
cultural capital experiences from an equity perspective. Every child will have a
champion who will be an advocate for that child. The champion will create a sense of
belonging by noticing, listening and supporting their child. Through a collective effort,
children will rise to the higher, consistent expectations to improve their academic and
wellbeing outcomes. This will also develop self-esteem and resilience to support
independent learning.
3 To ensure the strong foundations are in place for reading, writing and maths so
basic skills are automatic: We will confront these challenges with targeted, high-
quality support to unlock every child’s ability to be confident in reading, writing and
maths. This helps free up space for children to develop new skills and learning. By
focusing on phonics, handwriting, number fluency, spelling and sentence construction,
gaps in attainment will become smaller between most vulnerable children and their
peers.
4 Additional learning needs impacting core subjects: For some pupils, additional
learning needs are slowing progress in reading, writing and maths. We are committed
to identifying these needs with precision and providing tailored support and
interventions that accelerate progress and build lasting confidence. Baseline
assessments at the start of each academic year are often lower than peers, including
lower starting points when joining the school. Without targeted support theses gaps in
attainment could become larger over time.

Driving equity and excellence for all.

Part B: Review of outcomes in the previous academic
year
Pupil premium strategy outcomes
This details the impact that our pupil premium activity had on pupils in the 2024 to 2025
academic year.
Challenge 1: Work in partnership with families to improve attendance/punctuality to be
at or above average.
Listening to the views of parents and understanding the underlying issues, we have worked
with families to improve attendance. This has included fully funded places at Breakfast Club,
earlier drop off time to help the child settle into the day before the other children are in and
working with a group of external providers through a project called Solution Circles, where
different providers offer advice and support to the family.
Attendance figures for 2024/2025:
PPG Non-PPG
Attendance 93% (increase of 9% from 96%
Sept 24 to July 25)
Challenge 2: School to strengthen opportunities to develop background knowledge and
cultural capital experiences from an equity perspective.
PPG children have funding to attend clubs through the Sports Premium/PPG fund. Pupil voice
is gathered annually from the PPG children to see which clubs they enjoy attending and to
discuss the clubs on offer. These range from yoga, dance, art, drama and sporting clubs.
External visits to the school for example authors and an illustrator enhance the love of reading.
PPG children were given a copy of the books. PPG children get free places to PTA events and
families are contacted by the office in advance to remind them of the events taking place and to
see if their child would like to attend. The school also funds all external trips for PPG children
Challenge 3: Baseline assessments at the start of the academic year lower than peers,
including lower starting points when joining the school. Without targeted support these
gaps in attainment could become larger over time.
Results from 2024/25 Statutory Assessments
Non-PPG % PPG % Victoria PPG % National
EYFS GLD 81 0 (2 pupils) 51.3
Phonic Screening 86.7 50 (2 pupils) 67
Context
EYFS- 1 child had EHCP and went to specialist provision after Reception and 1 child joined us
at the end of May 2025. Both are SEND
PSC-1 child had an EHCP and both are SEND.
Termly Pupil Progress Meetings identify the impact of progress termly. PPG children have
made better progress in reading since the introduction of Little Wandle phonics. Maths and
writing still need to be more targeted with resources and scaffolding available, progress has
been made in these subjects, but they have not met expectations in their year groups.
Challenge 4: To develop self-esteem/resilience to support independent learning.
Using PSHE Association and 3D PSHE, all children are taught about relationships, overcoming
barriers and where to ask for help and support. During lessons, PPG children are supported
through QFT, scaffolding and questioning to enable them to become independent learners.
CPD for staff has been around QFT including Rosenshine’s Principles of Instruction on how to
8

PPG | Non-PPG

Attendance | 93% (increase of 9% from
Sept 24 to July 25) | 96%

Non-PPG % | PPG % Victoria | PPG % National

EYFS GLD | 81 | 0 (2 pupils) | 51.3

Phonic Screening | 86.7 | 50 (2 pupils) | 67

enable learners to be successful including breaking tasks into small steps, retrieval practice
and questioning. This has improved resilience in the classroom when approaching new and
unfamiliar tasks. It has also enabled PPG to make small steps of progress from their baseline
starting points.
There has also been staff training on adaptative teaching, where staff have identified specific
barriers to access the learning and put in place adaptations to support success.
Every class has a provision map for all staff to access which highlights the barriers to learning
and support to enable PPG children to access the curriculum successfully.
All KS1 classes use of the Zones of Regulation to enable children to be aware of their feelings
and how to manage them. (EYFS use a ‘emotions’ wall)
Challenge 5: Some pupils have special educational needs, in addition to being pupil
premium.
Nationally, 25.7% of children are eligible for FSM.22 However, this figure is 39.3% for those
categorised as receiving SEND support and 43.8% for those in receipt of EHCPs.23 While
overall children eligible for FSM are over-represented in the SEND cohort-Sutton Trust Report
2025
Early identification through baseline assessment and pupil progress information, enables staff
to pinpoint specific barriers to accessing core subjects. Since introducing Little Wandle, 1 child
has been making significant progress in their reading by following the SEND pathway, reducing
cognitive overload by simplifying the lesson structure. This is delivered 1:1. Another child has
been supported externally by a group of professionals through a program called ‘Solution
Circles’. This has all the different providers working together to improve attendance because of
arriving late to school. Since starting this, the child has been arriving at school most days just
after school starts, so has been present for the maths and English lessons. This has decreased
the attainment gap between non-FSM/SEND children.
By working closely with families, we have improved attendance, helped secure a place in a
special school for 1 child and narrowed the gaps in learning for core subjects.
We have reviewed our strategy plan and made changes to how we intend to use some of
the budget this academic year.

[SOURCE: VIC_SAFEGUARDING Report Autumn 2025.docx | Ofsted 26 Victoria /KEY DOCS | Modified: 2026-01-13]

Governing bodies, proprietors and management committees have a strategic leadership responsibility for their school or college’s safeguarding arrangements and must ensure that they comply with their duties under legislation. They must have regard for Keeping Children Safe in Education (DfE 2023) ensuring policies, procedures and training in their schools or colleges are effective and always comply with the law. Paragraph 78 KCSiE 2023

Sections 175 and 157 of the Education Act 2002 places a statutory duty on maintained and independent schools to make arrangements to ensure that in discharging their functions, they have regard to the need to safeguard and promote the welfare of children.

Governing bodies and proprietors should be aware of their obligations under the Human Rights Act 1998, the Equality Act 2010, (including the Public Sector Equality Duty), and their local multi-agency safeguarding arrangements, Hertfordshire Safeguarding children Partnership procedures manual.

This annual/half termly report to governors / trustees is an account of the whole school’s safeguarding training schedule, an update on school policies and procedures in relation to safeguarding and promoting the welfare of children/young people, an update of the progress made since the last reporting period and submission of safeguarding policy and practice and clarifying the processes for the review and monitoring of safeguarding arrangements. It also summarises the number of individuals for whom early help support and child protection casework has been undertaken.

Ofsted | Inspection Data Summary Report Skip to main content GOV.UK Sign out Inspection Data Summary Report Print preferences Select to show or hide guidance text and/or accompanying charts in print outputs Hide guidance text Hide accompanying charts Home IDSR Victoria Church of England Infant and Nursery School Download this page Release date: 12 December 2025 IDSR news page IDSR guidance Important information The IDSR summarises and analyses the data (statistical information) that is available about a school. It is designed to be used alongside our school inspection toolkit , to guide inspectors’ conversations with school leaders. It is not meant to provide a complete picture of a school or any judgement or assessment of a school. The IDSR might contain sensitive information about schools and colleges. It is your responsibility to make sure you store and share the IDSR securely. Please see the IDSR conditions of use and storage statement in our guidance (linked at the top of the IDSR). Data is presented in the IDSR based on the URN to which it is assigned. Where a school’s URN has changed within the timeframes represented in the IDSR, data will be displayed in different reports. School details School details URN: 117423 LAESTAB: 9193314 Local authority: Hertfordshire Phase of education: Primary Type of education: Voluntary Aided School Ofsted: explore an area Get information about this school (GIAS) Contents Context of the school Staffing Attendance and behaviour Links to alternative provision and other providers Achievement in phonics Report card Context of the school School characteristics Ethnicity SEN Year group School characteristics Data source: the DfE’s January school census for 2025, 2024 and 2023 Guidance The chart shows school-level and local area-level information for the last 3 years. For a school that has a sixth form, an additional row will show information relating to Years 12, 13 and, where applicable, 14. For each measure, text and shading are used to show where the school sits within the national distribution of all schools, or to show the banding of the local area average within the national distribution of all schools. The local averages are based on the DfE spring census of pupils at schools in the same phase. Local area averages are not shown for the school number on roll, location deprivation or stability measures. Local area is based on the middle-layer super output area (MSOA) in which the school resides. MSOAs comprise between 2,000 and 6,000 households and have a resident population of, generally, between 5,000 and 15,000 people. MSOAs fit within local authorities. The MSOA used in the IDSR for each school will be displayed at the top of this section. For more information about MSOAs, visit the Office for National Statistics website . The possible National distribution bandings in this section are: Well above average (blue shading) Above average Close to average Below average Well below average (orange shading) The banding shows how the school or local average figure compares with the national figure. If the value is more than 0.5 standard deviations (SDs) above the national rate, it is classed as ‘Above average’. If it is more than 0.5 SDs below the national rate, it is classed as ‘Below average’. If it is more than one SD above the national rate, it is classed as ‘Well above average’ and highlighted in blue. If it is more than one SD below the national rate, it is classed as ‘Well below average’ and highlighted in orange. Otherwise, it is ‘Close to average’ and shown in grey. Except for the school location deprivation measure, schools are compared with other schools in the same phase: primary (including middle deemed primary) or secondary (including middle deemed secondary, and all-through schools). Special schools are compared with the national distribution for secondary schools. FSM6 in this chart refers to the percentage of pupils who are or have been eligible for free school meals and have claimed them during Reception to Year 11 sometime in the last 6 years. Stability is a measure of the percentage of students who were admitted to the school at the standard time of admission. The stability percentage is calculated by dividing the number of pupils who meet the stability criteria by the number of all eligible pupils (pupils in Years 1 to 11 with a single or main dual registration at the school at the time of the January school census). The stability measure is not available for school sixth forms. Information regarding the level of deprivation in the school’s location is provided, along with aggregated pupil-derived deprivation indicators at school, sixth form (where applicable) and local area level. Each deprivation indicator is based on the English indices of deprivation from 2025. You can read further information about the data used for the local area deprivation . For deprivation measures, ‘Above average’ means “more deprived” and ‘Below average’ means “less deprived”. There is additional guidance about this section. The 2 halves of the table represent pupils who have SEN support and pupils who have an education, health and care (EHC) plan. No highlighting is applied to this table; figures are presented purely for information. The text above the table displays if the school has any resourced provision. This could be a SEN unit, resourced provision or both. The capacity represents how many pupils the provision displayed can accommodate. The type of SEN provision lists, where applicable, the type of SEN that is provided for. This section also displays how many pupils with SEN have received free school meals at any time during the last 6 years and/or who are looked-after children (in the care of the local authority for a day or more or who have been adopted from care). There is additional guidance about this section. Type of resourced provision: No resourced provision Capacity: Type of SEN provision: Number of pupils with SEN who are also FSM6 and/or CLA: 1 SEN primary need SEN support (11) R Y1 Y2 Total Hearing Impairment 0 0 1 1 Autistic Spectrum Disorder 1 2 0 3 School Support NSA 1 2 4 7 Year group totals 2 4 5 11 SEN primary need EHC plan (4) R Y1 Y2 Total Social, Emotional and Mental Health 0 1 0 1 Autistic Spectrum Disorder 1 2 0 3 Year group totals 1 3 0 4 Year group Data source: the DfE’s January school census for 2025 Guidance This table shows information about pupil characteristics for each year group. The table presents data for pupils who were recorded as part of the previous January school census. As such, the data presented against each year group is about the cohort that was in that year group in the last academic year. For example, the data in the Year 7 column is about the cohort who were in Year 7 in the previous academic year, not the cohort who are in Year 7 in the current academic year. The measures included in the table are: the number of pupils on roll the percentage of pupils in receipt of free school meals (FSM) at the time of the census; these pupils are those who are or have been eligible for FSM and have claimed them, during Reception to Year 11, sometime in the last 6 years (FSM data is not collected for sixth forms) the percentage of pupils whose first language is not English or is believed to be other than English the number of pupils identified as young carers the number of pupils who are children in need, including children on child protection plans and looked-after children – this includes children who have child-in-need plans and other types of plans or arrangements, and children waiting for a referral to be considered, an assessment to start, or an assessment that has started to be completed; these are pupils who were a child in need at any point during the reporting year (1 April to 31 March inclusive) the number of looked-after children – these are pupils who are looked after for at least one day during the year and are aged 4 to 15 at 31 August; this excludes looked-after children under an agreed series of short placements and those who were on remand/committed for trial or sentence and accommodated by the local authority No highlighting is applied to this table; figures are presented for information only. There is additional guidance about this section. The areas covered are: the proportion of education support staff relative to teaching staff teacher absence staff retention Information on staff turnover is calculated using the number of full-time equivalent (FTE) turnover leavers and the total FTE staff at the school. A school is considered to have high staff turnover if its turnover rate was in the highest 20% in any of the previous 3 years. Low staff turnover is not highlighted. Special schools are compared with primaries. Turnover and leavers at a school level can be very volatile from year to year. When looking at rates of leavers and turnover, it is important to consider school size, because this has a particularly big impact on turnover rates for small schools. There is additional guidance about this section. There is nothing to highlight for the proportion of education support staff relative to teaching staff in 2024. There is nothing to highlight for teachers with at least one period of sickness absence. There is nothing to highlight for days lost to teacher absence (6 days) in 2023/24. At the time of the November 2024 census, there were no full-time vacant teacher posts in the school. Staff turnover was in the highest 20% in: 2023, 2022, 2021. Back to contents Attendance and behaviour Attendance and persistent absence Data source: the DfE’s 2024/25 (2 term), 2023/24 (3 term), 2022/23 (3 term) and 2018/19 (3 term) academic year data Guidance This section contains tables and charts for overall attendance and persistent absence, which provide data for the school and a comparison with the national average. To compare the school value with the national average, we have calculated standard deviations (SDs) and confidence intervals (CIs). CIs are used for persistent absence only. Each set of tables and charts is available for 3 different pupil groups: all pupils enrolled at the school free school meals (FSM) pupils: those who have been eligible for FSM at any time during the last 6 years – referred to as FSM6 pupils who have a statement of special educational needs (SEN) or an education, health and care (EHC) plan, or who receive SEN support but do not have an EHC plan This section includes data for 2018/19 because, nationally, attendance declined sharply in 2020/21 during the COVID-19 pandemic and has not recovered. Inclusion of 2018/19 data will show whether the school has managed to return to pre-pandemic attendance levels. Overall attendance is calculated as: 100 – ((number of absence sessions / number of sessions it was possible to attend) * 100). Absence is the total of all authorised and unauthorised absences. Schools are compared with other schools in the same phase of education: primary (including middle deemed primary) or secondary (including middle deemed secondary and all-through schools). Special schools are compared against the national distribution for secondary schools. The national distribution banding shows how the school’s rate compares with the national rate. For overall attendance, if the school’s rate is more than 0.5 SDs above the national rate, it is classed as ‘Above’ and highlighted in green. If the school’s rate is more than 0.5 SDs below the national rate, it is classed as ‘Below’ and highlighted in red. Otherwise it is ‘Close to average’ and shown in grey. Due to the methodology used to calculate overall attendance, this banding does not indicate statistical significance. For persistent absence it is calculated differently, as it uses both the SD and a CI to indicate whether the school’s persistent absence rate is, statistically, significantly above or below the national average. School values that are more than 0.5 SDs above the national rate and significantly above average – Above (sig+) – are highlighted red. School values that are more than 0.5 SDs below the national rate and significantly below average – Below (sig-) – are highlighted green. All other values are shown in grey. Overall attendance trend shows whether the school trend is in line with the national trend, or if the school’s attendance has improved or declined relative to the national change. Persistent absence trend indicates whether there has been a statistically significant increase or decrease in the school’s persistent absence rate in a particular year, compared with the previous year. The year 3 rate is compared with 2018/19 to illustrate how persistent absence has changed since the COVID-19 pandemic. The school context column indicates whether the whole school has a higher-than-average proportion of students who have been eligible for FSM at any time during the last 6 years (FSM6), or a higher-than-average proportion of pupils who receive SEN support, or who have a statement of SEN or an EHC plan. There is additional guidance about this section. Key to charts Attendance Persistent absence Attendance The trend analysis for the Attendance measure is different to the trend analysis elsewhere in the IDSR. For Attendance, our trend analysis compares the school trend with the national trend and determines whether it is in line with it or whether the school trend is showing either relative improvement or relative decline. 2022/23 (3 term) 10 20.0% 25.7% Close to average (non-sig) No sig change - 2018/19 (3 term) 8 25.0% 14.8% Above (non-sig) Not available - Chart Suspensions and permanent exclusions - whole school Data source: the DfE’s 2023/24, 2022/23 and 2021/22 academic year data Guidance This section contains tables and charts for suspensions and permanent exclusions. These provide data for the school and a comparison with the national average. The number and proportion of pupils who have had suspensions or permanent exclusions in a school are often very small, especially in primary schools. These figures should be interpreted with caution, particularly when making comparisons over time. The suspensions and permanent exclusions data is one year behind and therefore may not relate to the same cohort as other IDSR data. The whole school measure includes all year groups in the school. This section contains tables and charts displaying information about pupils who have had one or more suspensions and pupils who have had two or more suspensions, for the most recent 3 years for which data is available. Each set of tables and charts is available for 3 different pupil groups: all pupils enrolled at the school free school meals (FSM) pupils: those who have been eligible for FSM at any time during the last 6 years – referred to as FSM6 pupils who have a statement of special educational needs (SEN) or an education, health and care (EHC) plan, or who receive SEN support but do not have an EHC plan To compare the school’s data with the national average, we have calculated standard deviations (SDs) and confidence intervals (CIs). Schools are compared with other schools in the same phase of education: primary (including middle deemed primary) or secondary (including middle deemed secondary and all-through schools). Special schools are compared against the national distribution for secondary schools. The national distribution banding shows how the school’s rate compares with the national rate. This comparison uses both the SD and a CI to indicate whether the school’s suspension rate is, statistically, significantly above or below the national average. School values that are more than 0.5 SDs above the national rate and significantly above average – Above (sig+) – are highlighted red. School values that are more than 0.5 SDs below the national rate and significantly below average – Below (sig-) – are highlighted green. All other values are shown in grey. Trend indicates whether there has been a statistically significant increase or decrease in the school’s suspension rate in a particular year, compared with the previous year. The school context column indicates whether the whole school has a higher-than-average proportion of students who have been eligible for FSM at any time during the last 6 years (FSM6), or a higher-than-average proportion of pupils who receive SEN support, or who have a statement of SEN or an EHC plan. Suspension rate data is positively skewed, especially for primary schools, because many schools have a very low suspension rate. However, a small number of schools have a higher rate, so there is a long above-the-national-average section, but only approximately a quarter of schools fall into this band. Some of these high rates may be due to errors in the source data. Reasons for suspensions and permanent exclusions are for the most recent year and they will only be displayed if there has been at least 1 suspension or permanent exclusion in the most recent year. Up to 3 reasons can be recorded. These reasons are recorded without weighting or prioritisation. Therefore, the total number of reasons may exceed the total number of suspensions or permanent exclusions. There is additional guidance about this section. URN UKPRN Companies House number Postcode Provider type Provider name Total pupils Full time Part time - - 15456230 BA1 3DU Company HOME-SCHOOL TUTORING HERTFORDSHIRE &amp; CAMBRIDGESHIRE LIMITED 1 (0) 0 (0) 1 (0) - - 14315805 HP23 5JD Company WILD KITES FOREST SCHOOL LTD 1 (0) 0 (0) 1 (0) Back to contents ") --> Characteristics of pupils who have moved schools ') --> ") --> Achievement in phonics Phonics screening check expected standard EYFS nationals Phonics screening check expected standard Data source: the DfE’s final 2025, final 2024 and final 2023 data Guidance This section contains a table and chart for the Year 1 phonics attainment measure, and sentences for both Year 1 and Year 2 phonics, which provide data for the school and a comparison with the national average. To compare the school value with the national average, we have calculated standard deviations (SDs) and confidence intervals. Data is presented for the most recent 3 years and is provided for all pupils only. We have averaged school and national results across data years to produce a 3-year average. This helps us smooth out year-to-year fluctuations in the data so we can spot genuine trends and significant patterns that might otherwise be hidden. This is particularly important in small schools, where a single pupil’s outcome can disproportionately affect the data. A school’s position on the national distribution is determined using SDs. ‘Above’ indicates when a school’s value is more than 0.5 SDs above the national value. ‘Below’ indicates when a school’s value is more than 0.5 SDs below the national value. All other school values are ‘Close to average’. Combining the school value’s position within the national distribution and a measure of statistical confidence in the school value, there are 7 possible interpretations of school performance (the national distribution banding). These are: Above (sig+) Above (non-sig) Close to average (sig+) Close to average (non-sig) Close to average (sig-) Below (non-sig) Below (sig-) When school values fall into the ‘Close to average’ banding, or they are not, statistically, significantly different from national (non-sig), the national distribution banding will be coloured grey. When school values fall into the ‘Above’ banding and are, statistically, significantly above the national value, it will be highlighted in green. When school values fall into the ‘Below’ banding and are, statistically, significantly below the national value, it will be highlighted in red. Trend shows whether there has been a statistically significant increase or decrease in the school’s value in a particular year, compared with the previous year. Trends are not available for the furthest year, as no comparator year is displayed. The year group context column indicates whether the relevant year group has a high proportion of pupils who are or have been eligible for free school meals (FSM) and have claimed them at some time in the last 6 years (FSM6), or have high special educational needs (SEN) or low stability. The phonics measure uses Year 1 cohort characteristics. The sentences show: the total number of pupils who were eligible to be screened for phonics the number of pupils who sat the check, and who did not, where applicable the number of pupils who did not meet the expected standard, where applicable the average mark for pupils who did not meet the expected standard (where the number not meeting the standard was greater than 5) The number of pupils who did not sit the phonics screening check includes (where applicable): the number who were absent, the number who were disapplied, and the number for whom there was maladministration. There is additional guidance about this section. Key to charts All pupils - Phonics expected standard There were 45 pupils who were eligible for the phonics screening check in Year 1 in 2025; 41 of them sat the check and 4 did not. Of those who sat the check, 2 pupils did not meet the phonics expected standard . There were 3 pupils who were eligible for the phonics screening check in Year 2 in 2025; all of them sat the check. EYFSP data is no longer externally moderated by local authorities, therefore should be treated with caution. Ofsted notes for governors
Every key document is in the Ofsted 25 folder on GovernorHub

subject leader reports / data / SIP / FIP

Boosting and intervention / CPD/ HfL advisors/ curriculum resources

The Governance Handbook assigns 3 core functions to the board:

Vision/ 
Values / Mustard Tree
5 year strategic plan

HR committee meeting – sample appraisal targets

Monitoring reports
Rationales for spending decisions
SFVS
Resources committee
The school is in a healthy financial position due to effective financial management and have a health
carry forward to weather the low birth rate pupil numbers
Spending decisions have been to improve pupil outcomes, CPD, effective curriculum resources and maintanance of the environment
Actions from the previous Ofsted:
The school does not give the
weakest readers the support they need. Pupils read
books that do not match precisely enough the sounds they know. This means that
pupils do not learn quickly to read with fluency. The school needs to review the
support given to early readers, and ensure that this is provided effectively, so that
the weakest readers remember their sounds better and can use these to build up
their skills and confidence in reading.
New phonics scheme – Little Wandle
2024 phonics screening check data 91% (national 80.3% /
Herts 81.3%)
External validation of impact – SEA visit / CSV visit
Forensic assessment / intervention / progress
The school does not use assessment as effectively as it could to check and ensure
that pupils remember what they have learned in the longer term. As a result,
some pupils lack the sticky knowledge they need to build up a detailed
understanding of their subjects. The school needs to ensure it uses the
information from checks on learning, both in lessons and at the end of units of
learning, to adapt the next curriculum delivery effectively. This will enable pupils
to remember more of what they learn in the longer term.
Deep dive methodologies
Recapping/ revisiting
Discussed at P&C 4/6/25
Pupil voices to check information is retained
Knowledge organisers
Not all staff know how to support pupils well, whether within lessons or outside.
They are not all clear on how to stop pupils falling behind, or how to successfully
support those who do. This is both in early reading and in some foundation
subjects. The school needs to ensure that all staff get the training and monitoring
they need to be able to support pupils effectively with their learning, so that the
implementation of the curriculum is more consistently effective.
CPD
Improvement plans
HR consultation
ECT mentoring
Boosting/ interventions
Safeguarding – created a culture of safeguarding where everyone is vigilant and everyone is responsible
Culture of vigilance
– deeply rooted and constantly revisited the welfare of
pupils is always the priority
