Incels
-
“involuntary celibates "online subculture of predominately young, heterosexual men who believe they are unable to establish relationships. These self-proclaimed incels tend to blame society – and women in particular – for their lack of sexual or romantic experiences. Andrew Tate culture
County lines
- County lines is a form of criminal exploitation. It is when criminals befriend children, either online of offline, and then manipulate them into drug dealing. The 'lines' refer to mobile phones that are used to control a young person who is delivering drugs, often to towns outside their home county.
Safer recruitment processes
– adverts/ shortlisting/social media checks/ references/application forms/ interview questions / references / gaps in timelines 
CPOMS
(online recording system for all staff) 
- recording / following up / 
every case or entry is monitored by another DSL and DDSL . Rigorous in record keeping and tenacious in following up concerns.
SINGLE CENTRAL RECORD
- is a mandatory document that educational establishments in the UK must maintain. It is a centralised record of all the pre-employment checks conducted on staff, including teachers, support staff and volunteers, to ensure they are suitable for working with children. The SCR is a legal requirement and a critical component of OFSTED/ISI inspections. During an inspection, the OFSTED/ISI Inspector will ask to view the record.

Collective worship – now has clear purpose and structure. Classroom worship is now relevant and engaging. “There exists a reverential atmosphere and high level of pupil attentiveness in Collective Worship, which is conducive to meaningful spiritual reflection.” Rev Pye – CW audit report.
All classrooms have a worship area, which the pupils can access as part of collective worship. 
Spiritual ambassadors have been established and taken an active role in worships and the school community. “As evidenced by discussion with the Spiritual Ambassadors group, pupils hold Collective Worship in high regard. They speak confidently about the purposes of Collective Worship, including how it enables them to meet as a community, to explore Bible stories, and to reflect, pray and sing together.” Rev Pye – CW audit report.

PSHE
L Freeman
SEA Visit | Audit completed of PSHE curriculum and where further resources were needed
PSHE Association resources and planning used to enhance 3D PSHE resources
Sequence of when to teach PSHE topics reviewed, then updated to ensure coverage and all staff informed
PSHE SL modelled PSHE lesson to KS1 teachers to show how to introduce ground rules, distance techniques and activities to enhance the teaching of PSHE
SEA Visit 
There have been further enhancements in provision since the last inspection. 
Teaching was observed to be strong and purposeful and all children were highly engaged in the thoughtful learning activities.  
The wider opportunities showcased and the response of the children through discussions demonstrate that they are very well prepared for the next stage in learning.  
The youngest children are well supported in learning environments with a range of thoughtful provocations to promote their interest and curiosity.  
Social skills were observed to be developed at such a young age.  They communicate their needs and wants clearly and have learnt routines and behaviours that support their emotional regulation.  
The aims in early years provision is to develop the characteristics of effective learning which include exploring, playing, concentrating, connecting, problem solving, experimenting and enjoying their activities – this was clearly in evidence. 
Staff model activities, expectations and behaviours well and empower the children, encouraging them to talk about their learning. 
Children are supported well to name their feelings which provides them with the vocabulary to support in conflict resolution.  
Independent learning strategies are highlighted along with the class agreement about rules and expectations – all strong examples of high-quality personal development. | Embed baseline/exit activities in all PSHE lessons
Continue to review and enhance the sequencing and progression of the PSHE provision (updating with latest trends relevant to age group)
Continue to review and embed the positive messaging abound diversity and protected characteristics across the curriculum

Reading 
L Freeman
SEA Visit
English Hub Half Termly | Reviewed assessment data for PPAMS and shared information at parent consultations. 
Website up to date, including links to Little Wandle, reading curriculum and tips for parents
Workshops for Little Wandle and PSC have been held for parents to support children at home, as well as understanding how we deliver the scheme.
Identified trends and gaps for addition phonic lessons in YR1 and repeated practice for blending in R.
YR2 children who have not completed the LW program continue with reading groups and rapid catch up. 
Regular assessments show children who need to Keep up are closing the gaps with the additional support.
Assessment
PSC- Predicted 86% YR1 and 100% YR2 retakes.
YR2 Assessment- 80% predicted to be expected or above 
All YR2 children have completed the Little Wandle program by Spring 2025.
Half termly assessment (every 3 weeks for children who are not on track) feeds into revisit lessons before moving onto new content and enables targeted support.
English Hub and SEA both reported the forensic and detailed approach to assessment to ensure gaps were filled and children developing fluency through targeted interventions.
SEA Visit
Exemplary progress is observed to have been made in response to this OFSTED key point for action.  The leaders and staff term deserve recognition for their commitment and determination to demonstrate high quality standards in this critical area of learning.  This visit demonstrated: 
Reading has been the highest priority.  Embracing the guidance from The English Hub has made the difference. 
There is an urgent, responsive approach if pupils start to fall behind in their phonic acquisition.  This is supported through the capacity, skilled and timely use of the Little Wandle tracking system.  
The weakest readers get effective support. This has been observed through the skilled delivery of the SEND programme with staff who have been trained comprehensively to deliver this to a high standard. 
Pupils read books that match the sounds they know. This means that pupils are learning to read quickly with fluency.
Reading for Pleasure 
Reading for pleasure is promoted through daily reading to class, author visits, weekly timetabled library sessions, range of genres of books in classrooms. 
Specialist reading weeks/days (World Book Day) focused on reading your own way, engaging children to read more and sharing their reading journey.
Connection with the local library through visits (EYFS) and reading challenges increased reading for pleasure.
Books are available at lunchtimes through our Little outdoor Library and at ASC. | SEA Recommendations
Further develop the role of the teaching assistants in relation to supporting learning development to ensure consistency of approach across all classrooms.  You have strong practitioners for are valuable assets to the school.  Aim for consistency in approach for the maximum impact on learning. 
Continue to develop parental engagement in the learning process for phonics and early reading.  You have many families who will want to engage and support their child’s learning and this is a further asset for the school.
English Hub support will continue with Little Wandle after ‘graduating’. This will ensure monitoring of consistency, updates and training to deliver Little Wandle.
Regular coaching/training sessions for staff to deliver best phonic lessons

[SOURCE: Ofsted notes on wellbeing.docx | Ofsted 26 Victoria /Governor Questions and Model Answers | Modified: 2025-06-18]

Demonstrating wellbeing support in school

At Victoria, wellbeing is a key priority embedded across all aspects of our culture.  We foster a supportive and inclusive environment where staff and pupils feel safe, valued, and heard.

Emotional wellbeing is promoted through open dialogue, consistent relationship building and a strong pastoral support.

We provide safe accessible spaces, and encourage regular checking in with form tutors and staff.

Wellbeing is also woven into the curriculum, e.g.: through PHSE, assemblies and subject links that promote emotional literacy, resilience, and empathy.

We regularly acknowledge national wellbeing events, such as MH awareness week and Anti-bullying week. .

The impact of our approach is evaluated through student surveys, attendance, behavior tracking and safeguarding data which inform targeted interventions.

Our student voice is central - wellbeing ambassadors and school councils help shape the support we offer.

A previous pupil questionnaire presented a positive perspective from pupils.  There was 100% agreement with most of the following statements, with few exceptions:

They are happy at school

They feel safe at school

They have fun at school

They are proud of the school

They value difference

They treat each other with respect

They are kind

They try hard to be resilient

They always have someone to talk to

We subscribe to the MH and WB charter and organise activities - informal coffee catchups, wellbeing boards, staff shout out.

Overall, our aim is to create a safe, nurturing environment where every learner can thrive academically and emotionally.

[SOURCE: OFSTED Inspection Preparation English DRAFT (1).docx | Ofsted 26 Victoria /Governor Questions and Model Answers | Modified: 2025-06-17]

OFSTED Inspection Preparation: English

Key Questions and Model Answers (DRAFT)

Ian Davies, Link Governor, English

Areas of focus

The October 2023 inspection highlighted three critical areas requiring improvement for English (phonics, reading, writing):

Weakest Readers Support: "The school does not give the weakest readers the support they need. Pupils read books that do not match precisely enough the sounds they know" 

Assessment Effectiveness: "The school does not use assessment as effectively as it could to check and ensure that pupils remember what they have learned in the longer term"

Staff Capability: "Not all staff know how to support pupils well. This is both in early reading and in some foundation subjects"

Evidence summary

The improvements implemented since the October 2023 inspection demonstrate measurable progress:

Phonics Screening Results: 91% pass rate in 2024, significantly above national and local authority averages

External Validation: School Effectiveness Adviser and local authority visits confirm improved provision – CONFIRM DATES

Consistent Teaching Quality: All classes now taught to a good standard, addressing previous variability 

Improved Knowledge Retention: Pupils demonstrating better recall and higher proportions achieving age-related expectations

These improvements provide evidence that the school has systematically addressed each of the key concerns raised in the original 'Requires Improvement' judgment, demonstrating the sustained improvement necessary for a positive re-inspection outcome.

Potential questions and model (suggested) answers

1. Early Reading and Phonics Support

Question: "How have governors ensured that addressing the weakest readers and phonics provision has become a strategic priority for the school, and what evidence can you show me of your involvement in setting this direction?"

Suggested Answer: Working closely with the headteacher, the Governing Board has supported rapid and effective change to deliver measurable improvement in all areas of concern outlined by the previous OFSTED report. The School Improvement plan was revised to ensure that supporting the weakest readers became the top strategic priority. Minutes from Governing Body meetings evidence the importance the board has placed on supporting the school staff to deliver improvement.

Question: "What specific changes have been made to ensure the weakest readers receive effective support, and how is the school ensuring reading books are precisely matched to the sounds pupils know?"

Suggested answer: Our 2024 Year 1 phonics screening check pass rate rose to 91%, exceeding the national 80.3% and Hertfordshire 81.3% averages. This demonstrates a marked improvement in early reading and has been validated by external reviews, with our School Effectiveness Adviser and a local authority commissioned visit noting significantly stronger phonics provision.

The School achieved this by implementing the Little Wandle phonics scheme, establishing a rigorous and consistent approach to early reading across the school. Pupils' reading books are now fully decodable and precisely matched to the sounds they have been taught, ensuring that even the weakest readers can practice with texts aligned to their phonics knowledge, addressing that particular concern. 

The school also introduced daily catch-up interventions and more forensic, frequent assessments to quickly identify any child falling behind, and this targeted support has rapidly boosted the fluency and confidence of our previously struggling readers.

Question: "How do you monitor the effectiveness of your phonics provision, and what evidence can you show me of improved outcomes for struggling readers?"

Suggested Answer: The school has implemented systematic monitoring procedures that track individual pupil progress through regular phonics assessments and reading fluency checks. Internal data shows that pupils can confidently recall key knowledge from earlier in the year, and a greater proportion of children are achieving age-related expectations in core subjects than previously [2].

2. Assessment and Knowledge Retention

Question: "How have you improved your assessment practices to ensure pupils remember what they learn in the longer term, and how do you use this information to adapt your curriculum delivery?"

Suggested Answer: Pupils are now retaining their learning much more effectively over time, and our internal data shows improved attainment as a result of improved assessment practices. Teaching staff have embedded a robust cycle of assessment and curriculum review - teachers now regularly recap prior learning and use quizzes and retrieval practice to strengthen long-term memory.

Subject leaders also conduct deeper reviews to monitor how well knowledge is sticking and to reinforce any content that pupils struggle to remember. Crucially, teachers now use assessment information far more effectively to inform their planning - they  are better-able to quickly identify gaps and revisit material as needed, ensuring new knowledge builds securely on what pupils have learned before.

Question: "Can you demonstrate how teachers use assessment information to identify and address gaps in pupils' phonics knowledge?"

Suggested Answer: The School has introduced more frequent assessments to quickly identify any child falling behind in phonics. Teachers now conduct regular sound checks and reading assessments that directly inform their daily planning [2]. When gaps are identified, immediate intervention is provided through daily catch-up sessions, aiming to ensure no child falls further behind.

3. Staff Training and Capability

Question: "What specific training have you provided to ensure all staff can effectively support pupils with early reading, and how do you monitor the impact of this training?"

Suggested Answer: Teaching is now more consistent across the school, and this is reflected in better support for pupils and improved progress in lessons. The latest monitoring found all classes being taught to a good standard, which is a notable step up from the variability seen at the time of the last inspection.

This improvement stems from robust action to raise staff skills - the school delivered targeted training to address gaps in subject knowledge and ensured new teaching strategies were embedded through follow-up monitoring. Where any teacher was struggling, individual improvement plans were put in place with clear expectations.

At the same time, the school bolstered support for new teachers through strengthened mentoring - pairing early career teachers with experienced mentors and introduced structured intervention programmes for pupils who need extra help, training our staff to deliver these effectively.

Question: "How do you ensure consistency in phonics teaching across all classes, and what monitoring systems do you have in place?"

Suggested Answer: The School has implemented the Little Wandle phonics scheme consistently across all classes, providing staff with a clear, structured approach to phonics instruction. Regular monitoring visits by senior leaders ensure fidelity to the programme, and ongoing coaching support is provided, where needed.

As governors, we have closely monitored all these measures and we can see the impact - teachers are now far more confident and effective in the classroom, and pupils receive much better support in both early reading and foundation subjects.

[SOURCE: Ofsted model questions and answers as discussed with SL Susan Holmes v4.docx | Ofsted 26 Victoria /Governor Questions and Model Answers | Modified: 2025-06-17]

Susan Holmes – Ofsted practice questions discussed with Maths SL at Victoria

From NGA Ofsted course

in terms of student development, what do you hope the curriculum will achieve by the time students leave this school 

This is the intent section of the subject action plan

We intend to create confident, resilient and independent mathematicians who transfer skills and knowledge to solve problems. We aim for children to achieve mastery in this subject by using a concrete, pictorial and abstract approach to enable them to delve deeper and develop their reasoning skills. Maths teaching at Victoria is creative and interactive, with practical resources to help children build their skills and understanding over time. Our values-based approach, underpinned by the school’s Christian vision, aims is to give them solid foundations, the curiosity to take their mathematical learning further and the resilience to embrace future mathematical challenges.

What do you know about how Maths is taught and how teachers are supported in delivering it

Described in Maths Subject Action Plan, discussed with subject lead at governor link visit 17/06/2025

Establish a consistent approach to teaching of maths through non-negotiables, high expectations and quality planning. 

Teachers to uplevel their knowledge and skills to ensure their vocabulary is accurate and clear.

Ensure teachers have a solid understanding of a fluency session and how to deliver it

Success criteria

Lesson observations demonstrate misconceptions are effectively addressed so children’s learning moves forward. 

Fluency sessions are fast paced, all children are engaged in learning and there are opportunities to revisit and deepen understanding. 

Working walls are used effectively to promote core learning and key vocabulary.

The SL monitors the teaching of Maths through lesson visits and book looks and gives constructive feedback.

How it is done:

Discussed with SL : many visits from TLAs: details summarised here

established non- negotiables for teaching maths. Lesson drop in with TLA to Cedar class. 

Learning walk carried out with Becky Roberts and Laura Dell 

TLA visit on 16.01.25 – Laura taught in both Year 1 classes to model pedagogy, manipulatives and R + R sheets. 

TLA led a staff meeting on 28.01.25 focused on R + R, I do – we do and CPA to uplevel knowledge and skills. TAs were invited to this staff meeting. 

Key features and techniques that we discussed:

Learn, practice (or rehearse), Apply

concrete, pictorial and abstract approach (CPA)

I do, we do, you do

In a "Maths Mastery" lesson using the "I Do, We Do, You Do" approach, the teacher first demonstrates the skill (I Do), then guides students through practice together (We Do), and finally allows students to apply the skill independently (You Do). 

Rehearsal and Reasoning sheets support many of the learning sequences. These provide children with the opportunity to gain more insight into the core ideas of each step.

Fluency, stem sentences, working walls, scaffolding

Stem sentences - The use of precise mathematical language is promoted-  Children are encouraged to speak in full sentences when they reply to questions. 

Keep up not catch up approach

These enable the children to develop fluency, problem-solving skills, and mathematical reasoning from an early age

Subject leader monitoring– Miss Gordon has done a Maths Mastery course and is rolling out ideas and methods from it across the school . She also does book looks, learning walks, pupil voice, lesson preparation checks to monitor the progress. TLAs – Teacher Learning Advisors -  visits occur throughout the year, approx. monthly

KS1 staff understand planning concepts, collaborative with SL

More generic answer

school uses well-established schemes -Hertfordshire ‘Essential Maths’ plans. Teaching is carefully sequenced, breaking down mathematical concepts into small, manageable steps, and ensuring that knowledge builds progressively from Reception through Year 2. Lessons typically follow a structure that includes practice, application, and reasoning, 

Teachers are well supported through a range of measures. They have access to high-quality planning and assessment resources, such as the HfL Essential Maths materials and Maths Mastery courses

The use of precise mathematical language is promoted-  Children are encouraged to speak in full sentences when they reply to questions. Assessment is continuous, partly using Pixl assessment—including pupil feedback, work scrutiny, and testing—to inform teaching and provide timely interventions where needed. 

what evidence do you look at to assess the impact of the curriculum on student outcomes

use of questioning in lesson; book looks, 

fluency sessions to overcome misconceptions

Stem sentences

Maths  mastery

SATS at KS1

Diagnostic papers, quizzes

Pixl has been used to aid transition to TC, won’t be used in future as not useful enough

Where there are gaps, interventions are planned

How do you support school leaders in addressing any challenges related to curriculum delivery or student progress 

Governor link visits termly, feedback after meetings with SEA, progress reports at governing board and committee meetings

How do you ensure that the curriculum remains relevant and adaptive to the changing needs  of students 

School follows the national curriculum. It stays relevant because it is what they have to teach. Refresher courses spring, summer, autumn to check any changes. Training sessions keep them up-to-date with the curriculum changes

Adaptive

Prioritise what needs to be taught

Work is done with scaffolding, maths in smaller steps. There is room in HFL course to spend longer on a topic if necessary, use smaller steps. Fluency exercises can guide this and prompt interventions. Pace appropriate to class

Keep up not catch up approach

[SOURCE: Finance questions.docx | Ofsted 26 Victoria /Governor Questions and Model Answers | Modified: 2025-06-17]

Finance questions

Question 1: How is the school’s budget managed and monitored, and how do you ensure spending aligns with the school’s priorities and development plan?


Our school’s budget is managed and monitored through a rigorous, transparent process that ensures all spending directly supports our school’s priorities, and in particular the objectives set out in our School Development Plan (SDP).

Budget Planning and Alignment with Priorities
Each year, the Headteacher and School Finance Lead draft the budget in close consultation with the Senior Leadership Team (SLT), ensuring that allocations are mapped explicitly to the school’s strategic priorities. For example, staffing levels have been maintained at Victoria for the coming year, despite the falling pupil numbers. The higher level of staffing will be directed towards boosting and intervention activities, in-line with the SDP.

During the annual budgeting process we also conduct multi-year financial planning, considering projected pupil numbers and staffing needs, to ensure the sustainability of our initiatives. For example in this year's process we discussed the falling birth rates and admission numbers quite substantially but we were still able to meet all current budget requests due to the strong financial position of the school. 

Governance and Oversight
The Resources Committee of the Governing Body meets at least four times per year to review detailed budget monitoring reports, additionally the full governing board also reviews these at least twice a year. These reports show actual expenditure against each budget line, highlight any variances, and provide narrative explanations. Governors are proactive in challenging spending decisions, ensuring they are evidence-based and linked to pupil outcomes. For example in February resources we discussed spending on subject specific budgets in particular as the finance reports showed an underspend of around £7k. We were reassured that all necessary funding was being made available to subject leads and were given the example of the ZooLab wow day which had been provided recently.

Accountability and Compliance
Governors annually review and approve financial policies and ensure compliance with the Schools Financial Value Standard (SFVS). All spending is subject to audit, and a clear trail of decisions is maintained. Our most recent accounts audit passed without any areas of concern.

Question 2: What financial skills and expertise does the governing board possess, and how do you ensure effective challenge and support in budget management?


As a finance governor, I can confirm that our governing board possesses a strong and diverse range of financial skills and expertise, which enables us to provide both effective challenge and support in the management of the school’s budget.

Financial Skills and Expertise on the Board
We conduct an annual skills audit using the National Governance Association (NGA) framework to identify the knowledge and experience within our team, including financial experience, and to highlight any gaps that need addressing through recruitment or training. 

We regularly access training through the local authority and the NGA to keep up to date with best practice in school financial management. Governors are supported through induction mentoring, and ongoing training refreshers to ensure all members are confident in their financial oversight role. We have one governor who specifically monitors governor training to ensure we are covering all of the areas needed.

Our board includes governors with professional backgrounds in finance, business management, legal, and charity management. For example I have 20+ years in financial services and banking, including running business units of banks, our chair is a qualified solicitor and the (retiring) Chair of our Resources Committee is the CEO of a charity. This breadth of expertise ensures we can interpret financial reports, understand funding streams, and contribute to strategic financial planning.

Ensuring Effective Challenge and Support
We ensure effective challenge and support in several ways:

Regular, Timely Financial Monitoring: The finance committee meets at least termly to review detailed, up-to-date budget monitoring reports prepared by the School Finance Lead. We scrutinise variances, discuss virements, and monitor the forecast year-end position for both revenue and capital. For example at our February meeting the governors questioned an overspend on the schools water budget. 

Linking Finance to Strategic Priorities: All financial decisions are explicitly linked to the school’s development plan and strategic objectives. Governors challenge leaders to demonstrate how spending proposals will impact pupil outcomes and school improvement. For example we in the February Resources Committee we discussed benchmarking data which showed the substantial investments being made in interventions and boosting activities.

Question 3: How do you ensure that the school achieves value for money in its spending, including procurement and staffing decisions?

To ensure the school achieves value for money in all spending—including procurement and staffing decisions—the governing board follows a structured, transparent approach rooted in best practice and continuous evaluation.

Procurement and Purchasing Decisions
The governors are closely involved in all strategic investment decisions. For example there is currently a sub-committee reviewing the sustainability approach for the schools. This includes looking at things like boiler efficiency, window and wall insulation and potential investments like solar panels. In considering these purchasing decisions the governors are actively discussing and balancing short-term investments needs with long-term bill reduction opportunities.

For these kinds of decisions (once approved in principle) we then operate a clear procurement policy that typically mandates obtaining at least three quotes for all purchases above a set threshold, ensuring competitive pricing and fair assessment of bids. When assessing quotes, we use defined award criteria that balance price, quality, and supplier reputation. For example we replaced our telephone system earlier this year and managed to do so with a high quality system from a reputable supplier whilst remaining below budget due to our robust procurement process.

Staffing and Workforce Management
Staffing is our largest area of expenditure, so we regularly review staffing structures to ensure they align with the school’s current and future needs, as outlined in the School Development Plan. We consider the cost-effectiveness of all roles and use benchmarking data to compare our staffing spend with similar schools. Where appropriate, we use flexible staffing solutions, such as one-year contracts, to manage short-term needs efficiently without compromising on long-term flexibility. This has been particularly true of our additional intervention and boosting staff - many of whom are on one-year contracts.

Question 4: Can you provide examples of how financial decisions have directly impacted pupil outcomes or the quality of education?


Financial decisions made by the governing board are always focused on improving pupil outcomes and the quality of education, and we regularly review the impact of our spending to ensure it is making a measurable difference.

Probably the most tangible example of this was as a result of the last ofsted inspection. Quickly following this the HoS Lisa identified the need to replace the existing reading scheme and to boost intervention activities. Resources were immediately made available for these additional investments. 

Targeted Investment in Learning Resources
More generally though we allocate funding each year to update and enhance learning resources, such as books, technology, and classroom materials. For example £5000 has been budgeted at Victoria for the coming year for curriculum resources and a further £3.5k for curriculum subscriptions. The impact of these investments is closely observed during our various link visits.

Staffing and Intervention Programmes
We prioritise funding for high-quality teaching and targeted intervention programmes, recognising that smaller class sizes and additional support staff can have a significant impact—especially for disadvantaged pupils. Despite the small class sizes at Victoria we have also been able to invest in specific intervention activities - such as additional reading support for the pupils who need it.


Professional Development
Investing in staff professional development is another key area. In particular in Victoria we have a couple of ECT’s who are actively mentored and supported by more senior staff members and who are progressing extremely well as a result.

Monitoring and Evaluation
All major spending decisions are reviewed for impact using pupil progress data, feedback from staff and pupils, and external benchmarks. Where spending does not have the intended impact, we reallocate resources to more effective strategies.


---
[SOURCE: Safeguarding Questions.docx | Ofsted 26 Victoria /Governor Questions and Model Answers | Modified: 2025-06-16]

Safeguarding Questions

How does the Governing Body assure itself that safeguarding is effective in the school?

The Governing Body receives regular safeguarding reports from the Designated Safeguarding Lead (DSL) which in our case is the Head of School, including updates on training, referrals, and policy implementation. We conduct an annual safeguarding audit as well as a termly review which also looks at the Single Central Record (SCR). The Safeguarding Link Governor meets with the DSL each term to review case studies and ensure procedures are followed. We also ensure all governors complete annual safeguarding training.

How do you ensure that safeguarding policies and procedures are up to date and followed?

Our safeguarding policy is reviewed annually and updated in line with the latest KCSIE guidance. The policy is ratified by the full Governing Body and shared with all staff. We monitor implementation through governor visits, staff feedback, and safeguarding audits. The DSL provides assurance that all staff have read and understood the policy, and we check this during link governor visits.

How do you know that staff are trained and confident in safeguarding procedures?

We receive termly reports on staff training, including induction for new staff and annual refresher training. The DSL ensures all staff complete training on KCSIE and Prevent. Governors verify this through training logs and discussions during visits. We also review the outcomes of any safeguarding incidents to ensure staff have responded appropriately and confidently. Governors also ask safeguarding related questions of staff during Governor visits.

How does the Governing Body ensure that pupils feel safe and know how to keep themselves safe?

We monitor pupil voice through surveys and discussions during governor visits. The curriculum includes PSHE and online safety education, and we ensure this is regularly reviewed. Governors also review safeguarding logs and behaviour data to identify trends. Feedback from pupils indicates they feel safe and know who to talk to if they have concerns.

What is your role in managing allegations against staff or safeguarding concerns involving adults?

We follow statutory guidance and the school’s safeguarding policy. The Chair of Governors is the designated point of contact for allegations against the Headteacher. We ensure that all allegations are reported to the Local Authority Designated Officer (LADO) and that procedures are followed rigorously. We also ensure confidentiality and support for all parties involved.

How do you ensure the Single Central Record (SCR) is compliant and up to date?

The SCR is reviewed termly by the Safeguarding Link Governor alongside the DSL and school business manager. We check that all required checks are recorded, including DBS, identity, qualifications, and right to work. Any anomalies are addressed immediately. We also ensure that the SCR is securely stored and accessible for inspection.

How do you monitor the effectiveness of safeguarding across the school, including online safety?

We receive regular updates on safeguarding trends, including online safety incidents, through the DSL’s reports (included in the Head of School report). The curriculum includes online safety education, and we review its impact through pupil voice and incident logs. Governors also review filtering and monitoring systems to ensure they are effective and compliant with DfE guidance.

How do you ensure that safeguarding is part of the school’s culture and not just a compliance exercise?

We promote a safeguarding-first culture by embedding it in all aspects of school life. This includes regular staff briefings, visible safeguarding displays, and open communication channels for pupils and staff. Governors observe this culture during visits and through feedback from staff and pupils. We also ensure safeguarding is a standing item on every governing board agenda.

How do you ensure that vulnerable pupils are identified and supported effectively?

We monitor the provision for vulnerable pupils through reports from the DSL, SENCO, and pastoral team. The Safeguarding Link Governor reviews case studies and anonymised data to understand how early help and external referrals are managed. We also ensure that staff are trained to identify signs of vulnerability and that interventions are timely and effective.

How do you evaluate the impact of safeguarding training for staff and governors?

We evaluate training impact through staff surveys, safeguarding audits, and incident reviews. Governors discuss training outcomes with the DSL and ensure that learning is applied in practice. We also reflect on safeguarding case studies to assess whether staff have responded appropriately and identify areas for further development.

How do you ensure that the school’s filtering and monitoring systems are effective in protecting pupils online?

We review the school’s filtering and monitoring systems termly to ensure they are robust and compliant with DfE guidance. The DSL provides reports on online safety incidents and the effectiveness of the systems in place. We also ensure that staff and pupils receive regular training on online safety and responsible internet use.

How do you ensure that online safety education is embedded in the curriculum?

Online safety education is integrated into the PSHE curriculum and reinforced through assemblies and workshops. We review the curriculum regularly to ensure it addresses current online risks and trends. Governors also seek feedback from pupils and staff to ensure the education provided is relevant and effective.


---
[SOURCE: Ofsted model questions and answers Susan Holmes v3.docx | Ofsted 26 Victoria /Governor Questions and Model Answers | Modified: 2025-06-14]

Susan Holmes – Ofsted practice questions

From NGA Ofsted course

Perplexity (write a school governor reply for a Hertfordshire school for EYFS and KS1 (ages 4 -7 ) to the question 'How do you support school leaders in addressing any challenges related to curriculum delivery or student progress'

what evidence do you look at to assess the impact of the curriculum on student outcomes 

We  look at a range of evidence to assess the impact of the curriculum on student outcomes:

Statutory Assessment Data:
We review outcomes from statutory assessments, such as the Early Years Foundation Stage (EYFS) Profile at the end of Reception, the Year 1 Phonics Screening Check, and end of Key Stage 1 assessments in reading, writing, and mathematics. These provide a benchmark for how well children are achieving against national expectations

Ongoing Teacher Assessment and Progress Data:
we consider teacher assessments and data from regular, in-school tracking ( Pixl for Maths and other subjects) , which show how well children are progressing through the curriculum. This includes both formative assessments (such as observations and in-lesson questioning) and summative assessments (such as quizzes and end-of-unit tasks) 

Work Scrutiny and Moderation:
We do book looks -  looking at samples of pupils’ work across the curriculum to check coverage, progression, and the depth of learning. 

Pupil Voice and Engagement:
Staff get feedback from children about what they have learned and how confident they feel, 

Subject Leader and Headteacher Reports:
we review regular reports from subject leaders and the headteacher, which summarise strengths, areas for development, and the impact of any changes or interventions on learning and progress. We have termly governor link visits.

External Reviews and Benchmarking:
We also look at findings from external reviews, such as local authority advisors, and compare school performance to local and national data to ensure our curriculum is having the intended impact for all groups of pupils

The inspection focussed on Maths ; what do you know about how this subject is taught and how teachers are supported in delivering it

Our school uses well-established schemes such as the Hertfordshire ‘Essential Maths’ plans. Teaching is carefully sequenced, breaking down mathematical concepts into small, manageable steps, and ensuring that knowledge builds progressively from Reception through Year 2. Lessons typically follow a structure that includes practice, application, and reasoning, 

Key features and techniques that we discussed:

Learn, practice (or rehearse), Apply

I do, we do, you do

In a "Maths Mastery" lesson using the "I Do, We Do, You Do" approach, the teacher first demonstrates the skill (I Do), then guides students through practice together (We Do), and finally allows students to apply the skill independently (You Do). 

Rehearsal and Reasoning sheets support many of the learning sequences. These provide children with the opportunity to gain more insight into the core ideas of each step.

These enable the children to develop fluency, problem-solving skills, and mathematical reasoning from an early age

Teachers are well supported through a range of measures. They have access to high-quality planning and assessment resources, such as the HfL Essential Maths materials, which provide detailed learning sequences and guidance for each year group. 

Subject leader monitoring– Miss Gordon has done a Maths Mastery course and is rolling out ideas and methods from it across the school . She also does book looks, learning walks, pupil voice, lesson preparation checks to monitor the progress. TLAs – Teacher Learning Advisors -  visits occur throughout the year

The use of precise mathematical language is promoted-  Children are encouraged to speak in full sentences when they reply to questions. Assessment is continuous, partly using Pixl assessment—including pupil feedback, work scrutiny, and testing—to inform teaching and provide timely interventions where needed. 

How do you ensure that the curriculum remains relevant and adaptive to the changing needs  of students 

we ensure the curriculum remains relevant and adaptive through a close partnership with school leaders and staff

Alignment with EYFS and Key Stage 1 Frameworks:
We check to ensure the curriculum fully meets the statutory requirements of the Early Years Foundation Stage (EYFS) for Reception (age 4-5) and the National Curriculum for Key Stage 1 (Years 1 and 2, ages 5-7). This guarantees a strong foundation in communication, literacy, numeracy, and personal development, while allowing flexibility to tailor learning to our pupils’ needs

Regular Curriculum Review and Adaptation:
The curriculum is reviewed frequently to reflect changes in government guidance, educational research, and the developmental needs of young children. This includes adapting teaching approaches to incorporate play-based learning in EYFS and more structured but still engaging learning in Key Stage 1

Staff Development and Innovation:
We try to ensure  ongoing professional development for teachers to keep up with best practices in early years and primary education, including new approaches to teaching foundational skills and fostering creativity and curiosity

Holistic Development:
Beyond academic skills, the curriculum promotes physical development, social skills, and emotional well-being, recognising the whole child’s needs at this formative stage

Through this approach, we ensure that our curriculum not only meets statutory requirements but also evolves to meet the unique and changing needs of our youngest learners, preparing them well for the next stages of their education.

How do you support school leaders in addressing any challenges related to curriculum delivery or student progress 

Strategic Oversight and Setting Direction

the governing body works to set high expectations and clear targets for pupil achievement, ensuring that the curriculum in EYFS and KS1 meets statutory requirements and reflects the needs of all learners

We regularly review the School Improvement Plan (SIP) and Self Evaluation Form (SEF) to identify priority areas and monitor progress against agreed objectives

Acting as a Critical Friend

We maintain a positive, supportive relationship with school leaders, meeting regularly to discuss progress, challenges, and areas for development.

We ask probing questions about pupil progress, the effectiveness of interventions, and how the curriculum is adapted to support all children, including those with additional needs1

we encourage leaders to reflect on what is working well and where further improvements can be made, particularly in early reading, phonics, and foundational skills essential for later learning

Monitoring and Accountability

The governing body receives regular reports from the headteacher and phase leaders on pupil attainment and progress data, including breakdowns by year group, subject, and key groups such as disadvantaged pupils and those with SEND

We scrutinise this information to ensure that gaps are identified early and that swift, effective action is taken to address underperformance

Governors participate in learning walks, book looks, and attend school events to see the curriculum in action and understand the pupil experience first-hand

Professional Development and Collaboration

We support leaders in accessing high-quality training and development opportunities, both through Hertfordshire’s governance services and local networks45.

Governors encourage collaboration with other local schools to share best practice and learn from successful approaches to curriculum delivery and raising standards5.

Championing All Pupils

The governing body ensures the curriculum is inclusive and broad, providing for the spiritual, moral, social, and cultural development of all children1.

We monitor the impact of strategies to support vulnerable groups and challenge leaders to ensure that every child in EYFS and KS1 is given the best possible start to their education12.

Summary Statement

By providing strategic direction, acting as a critical friend, ensuring robust accountability, and championing professional development, we help ensure that every child receives a high-quality education and achieves their full potential

can you give examples of how the board has responded to any areas where students may not be achieving as expected 

School Governor Response: Board Actions When Students Are Not Achieving as Expected (EYFS and KS1)

Data Scrutiny and Challenge

The board regularly reviews detailed pupil progress and attainment data, provided termly by the headteacher and senior leadership team. When data shows that certain groups—such as disadvantaged pupils or those with SEND—are not meeting expected standards, governors challenge leaders to explain the reasons and outline actions being taken to address these gaps

We place particular scrutiny on the progress of vulnerable groups and the effective use of Pupil Premium funding to ensure targeted support is in place for those most at risk of underachievement

Policy Review and Implementation

Governors annually review and approve key policies, including those related to teaching, learning, and inclusion. We ask the headteacher to provide real examples of how these policies have been enacted to support pupils who are not achieving as expected, ensuring that policy translates into effective practice

Performance Management and Target Setting

The governing board is actively involved in the headteacher’s performance management, setting and reviewing targets that often include specific objectives for improving outcomes in EYFS and KS1. We also scrutinise staff performance management reports to ensure that all teachers are held accountable for pupil progress and that support or training is provided where needed

Commissioning Additional Support and Interventions

When underachievement is identified, the board supports leaders in accessing additional resources or expertise. For example, we have encouraged participation in Hertfordshire’s Early Years Diminishing the Difference programme and the Early Years Transition Project, both designed to raise attainment for children with low starting points and improve transitions into school

We have also supported the use of external advisors, such as Hertfordshire Improvement Partners, to provide guidance and challenge around closing attainment gaps

Workforce Development 

The board has endorsed further training for staff on effective intervention approaches and transition guidance, ensuring that children at risk of falling behind receive the right support at the right time

Monitoring Impact

Governors monitor the impact of all interventions and strategies through follow-up data reviews, learning walks, and meetings with staff. Where progress remains insufficient, we work with leaders to adapt approaches and, if necessary, allocate additional resources or seek further external support

In summary, the board takes a proactive and systematic approach to addressing areas where students may not be achieving as expected, combining robust data analysis, policy oversight, targeted intervention, and ongoing monitoring to ensure every child in EYFS and KS1 is supported to reach their full potential

in terms of student development, what do you hope the curriculum will achieve by the time students leave this school

--- Slide 17 ---
Cultural capital
178. As part of making the judgement about the quality of education, inspectors will consider the extent to which schools are equipping pupils with the knowledge and cultural capital they need to succeed in life. Our understanding of ‘knowledge and cultural capital’ is derived from the following wording in the national curriculum: 
‘It is the essential knowledge that pupils need to be educated citizens, introducing them to the best that has been thought and said and helping to engender an appreciation of human creativity and achievement.’ 

--- Slide 18 ---

The world can be an exciting place full of music, art, places to go, people to find out about, sports to do and experiences to have. 
We all have a cultural capital – we can expand this.

Regardless of a child’s background, there may be gaps.

[SOURCE: Governance in the Context of Leadership.docx | Ofsted 26 Victoria /Linda (SEA) Ofsted Prep resources | Modified: 2025-07-01]

Governance in the Context of Leadership

Causing Concern

Governors/trustees do not carry out their statutory core functions.

Their contribution to the school’s strategic aims is weak.

There may be serious breaches of legal responsibilities that negatively impact pupils.

Leaders may fail to identify or act on key issues, and there is no track record of improvement.

Secure

Governance has an accurate understanding of the school’s context, strengths, and weaknesses.

This understanding informs the actions they take.

Leaders’ actions are well judged and consistently effective.

Strong

Governors/trustees challenge and support leaders effectively.

They monitor the impact of decisions and ensure accountability.

Governance contributes to a clear strategic direction and improvement planning.

Exemplary

Governance is embedded and sustained over time.

It makes a tangible difference to pupils’ learning, development, and well-being.

Governance practices are used or adapted internally to improve other areas of the school.

These practices are subject to moderation and confirmation by the national quality and consistency panel.


---
[SOURCE: V Governance questions 2025.docx | Ofsted 26 Victoria /Linda (SEA) Ofsted Prep resources | Modified: 2025-07-01]

Drawing from the Education Inspection Framework (EIF), the 2025 Draft OFSTED Toolkit and the Governance Handbook, here is a set of top-level questions an OFSTED inspector might ask a team of governors to evaluate the effectiveness of governance in an English primary school:

🏛️ Strategic Leadership and Vision

How do you ensure the school’s vision and values are clearly defined, shared, and embedded in practice?

What is your understanding of the school’s strengths and areas for development?

How do you hold leaders to account for the quality of education and pupil outcomes?

📊 Monitoring and Evaluation

How do you monitor the impact of the curriculum and teaching on pupil progress?

What systems do you use to evaluate the effectiveness of school improvement strategies?

How do you ensure that data and evidence inform your decisions?

🧑‍🎓 Pupil Outcomes and Inclusion

How do you ensure that all pupils, including those with SEND and disadvantaged backgrounds, are supported to achieve well?

What evidence do you have that the school is closing gaps in attainment and progress?

🧠 Safeguarding and Well-being

How do you assure yourselves that safeguarding is effective and compliant with statutory guidance?

What role do you play in promoting pupils’ mental health and well-being?

📚 Curriculum and Personal Development

How do you ensure the curriculum is broad, balanced, and ambitious for all pupils?

What oversight do you have of the school’s provision for personal development, including SMSC and British values?

👥 Staff Development and Workload

How do you support and challenge school leaders in managing staff workload and well-being?

What is your role in ensuring high-quality professional development for staff?

📈 Leadership Capacity and Improvement

How do you evaluate the effectiveness of the leadership team?

What evidence do you have of sustained improvement or capacity to improve?

📑 Compliance and Statutory Duties

How do you ensure compliance with statutory responsibilities, including safeguarding, SEND, and equality?

How do you fulfil your core functions: strategic direction, holding leaders to account, and financial oversight?

💬 Stakeholder Engagement

How do you engage with parents, carers, and the wider community?

What mechanisms are in place for gathering and responding to stakeholder feedback?

Primary Assessment Pro le
EYFSP Early Learning Goals - Victoria CofE Infant & Nursery School
This pro le shows the achievements on all early learning goals for all Early Years pupils at Victoria CofE Infant &
Nursery School.
The early years foundation stage pro le was signi cantly revised in September 2021. This included changes to the
17 early learning goals and 7 areas of learning.
For a higher level view, please see the Good Level of Development (GLD) and Areas of Learning pro le.

Please note:
We advise caution over the use of school performance data since the pandemic, as this had an uneven impact on di erent schools and
pupils. The data is best used as a starting point for discussion, for example to explore the impact of Covid on pupil performance.
There were no published  gures for 2020 or 2021, as statutory assessment was cancelled in these years due to Covid.
Data is suppressed for pupil groups of 5 or fewer and the complementary group, e.g. Disadvantaged and Non-disadvantaged.
‘Disadvantaged’ relates to pupils who are eligible for Free School Meals or have been eligible at any point in the last 6 years.

[SOURCE: Victoria CE Infant and Nursery School - BA focus 19032025.docx | Ofsted 26 Victoria  | Modified: 2025-05-09]

SEA Visit Report

School | Victoria CE Infant and Nursery School | Number | 912 | Date | 19th March 2025

Present: | Lisa Freeman, Head of School; Rebecca Roberts, Executive Headteacher; Linda Hardman, District School Effectiveness Lead for Dacorum.  Feedback to a member of the governing body. | Lisa Freeman, Head of School; Rebecca Roberts, Executive Headteacher; Linda Hardman, District School Effectiveness Lead for Dacorum.  Feedback to a member of the governing body. | Lisa Freeman, Head of School; Rebecca Roberts, Executive Headteacher; Linda Hardman, District School Effectiveness Lead for Dacorum.  Feedback to a member of the governing body. | Lisa Freeman, Head of School; Rebecca Roberts, Executive Headteacher; Linda Hardman, District School Effectiveness Lead for Dacorum.  Feedback to a member of the governing body. | Lisa Freeman, Head of School; Rebecca Roberts, Executive Headteacher; Linda Hardman, District School Effectiveness Lead for Dacorum.  Feedback to a member of the governing body.

Length of visit: Half a day plus preparation and report writing (Spring visit 2)

Purpose of visit and activities undertaken: To review the quality of behaviour and attitudes across the school.
This visit consisted of:
Discussion with leaders about key policies relating to behavioural matters and the implementation across the school 
Exploration of recording of incidents on the school’s online behaviour monitoring system (CPOMS)
Exploration of the Behaviour Curriculum
Visit classes to observe behaviour for learning in practice
Review of attendance data, systems and processes
Review of case studies including risk reduction and behaviour plans
Discussion with an ECT regarding support provided in relation to ensuring high quality behaviour and attitudes.
A discussion with a small group of children about matters relating to behaviour and attitudes
A review of information relating to behaviour and attitudes on the school website 
Feedback to a member of the governing body

Context
Victoria CE Infant and Nursery School was last inspected in October 2023 and the strengths in behaviour and attitudes was explicitly highlighted in the report:
“Victoria is a safe and orderly place. Pupils are happy here. They enjoy positive relationships with caring staff. Children in the early years learn and play in a stimulating environment. They build their curiosity and independence. Pupils understand and embody the school’s values.”
“There are high expectations of behaviour. This helps pupils focus in lessons. They behave well in breaks and as they walk around the school. Pupils respond well to clear routines. They learn to be considerate, such as through the ‘Kindness Week’.”
“The school has a well-considered behaviour policy. Leaders have communicated this carefully to staff and pupils. As a result, the policy is consistently applied. Any incidents get resolved quickly. Leaders deal with the underlying causes of misbehaviour and low attendance. Therefore, pupils attend and behave well.”
Information taken from the current Inspection Data School Report (IDSR) shows that attendance levels at Victoria CE Infant and Nursery School over the past three years have continued to improve over time.  The most recent published data shows that attendance and persistent absence is better than the national average.


Information on this database is historic but is used by the inspectorate to gain a picture of outcomes over time.
There were 5 suspensions recorded in 2022-2023 (latest published data). The reason for the suspensions was physical abuse towards adults.  
The data on the IDSR shows that 13 pupils are in receipt of SEND support. Primary presenting strands include speech language and communication needs (SLCN), autistic spectrum diagnosis (ASD) and physical disability (PD).  The largest group have non-specific presenting needs.
The school website represents well information relating to the strands relating to behaviour and attitudes. Policies are robust, regularly updated, relevant and in line with statutory responsibilities.  Of particular note is the Vision and Values statement, Attendance policy, Behaviour Ladder, E safety guidance, Golden Rules, and information relating to the work of the Kindness and Spiritual ambassadors.  These set the tone for the high expectations around behaviour, attitudes and ethos at the school.

Additional observations: 
Leaders consistently prioritise positive behaviour and attitudes and the strengths of provision in this area are transparent and have been enhanced still further through actions taken by the leaders to rapidly improve the quality of education in the school and further widen and enrich the personal development offer.  The strands of behaviour and attitudes and the high quality curriculum offer align.  Children are engaged and motivated, want to learn and so their behaviour for learning is consistently observed to be of a high standard in this school.
The pupil questionnaire administered earlier in the school year presents a positive perspective from the pupils in relation to their behaviour and attitudes. There was 100% agreement with most of the statements below, with few exceptions.  For example:
They are happy at the school
They feel safe at school
They have fun at school
They are proud of the school
They value difference
They treat each other with respect
They are kind
They are thankful
They try hard to be resilient
They have someone to talk to
They know the difference between right and wrong and how to respond if something is wrong
They follow the school values and model them to others
They understand how to make others feel welcome
They understand if they are unkind this can make others upset
They understand the importance of having rules
They understand the consequences of their behaviour and actions.
The analysis of absence is forensic and monitored with precision.  Leaders are strict about not authorising absence beyond the stated circumstances in the school’s revised Attendance policy (last reviewed September 2024 in line with the statutory guidance.)  Reasons for absence are ascertained and appropriate support is provided through the Family Support Worker and EBSA(emotionally based school avoidance) strategies for specific children and their families according to the presenting need.  At the time of the visit there were 8 children who were being closely monitored as persistent absentees.  One child was in receipt of alternative provision.  This has been carefully planned and is being tenaciously monitored for impact.  This has proved to be challenging on account of a lack of communication from other parties working in support of the child.

Discussion with pupils
Pupils have a positive view of their life at the school.  They like the sport and exercise opportunities to keep them healthy.  They enjoy making friends, learning new things and very much appreciate “ having such lovely teachers.”  
Their favourite parts of the school day include playing together, having the school lunches, their PE lessons and learning mathematical facts.  The explained that pupils behave very well in lessons.  There is often lots of laughter at lunchtime.  It is, from their perspective, a happy place to be.  They demonstrate a clear understanding of what happens if someone chooses not to follow the rules.  They explained the possible consequences and say that if there are any problems the matters get resolved very quickly.  They believe it is important to be a good friend to others and the most important elements of this is to be both honest and kind.  The children explain that there are good attitudes for learning at the school.  Everyone focuses on what the teacher is saying because they do not want time out.  They explained that fiddle toys are helpful and enable some children to concentrate.  They demonstrate perseverance and resilience in their learning.  They say they feel proud, happy and delighted when they try to do something difficult and get it right.  They explain that they take a pride in all aspects of their school and they feel that  having certificates, merits and a level of competition helps them to concentrate and want to achieve the very best they can in their learning.  Their social interactions are well developed and supported.  The pupils have a positive view of their school rules and understand that they are there to keep everyone safe.  They appreciate that teachers help and guide them and support them to understand the mistakes they have made when they have made poor choices in relation to behaviour. They know how to support a child who might appear to be upset or lonely.  A wonderful part of this discussion was when one child  explained the importance of “paying it forward” when people are kind to them.  The breadth of this discussion with the  children demonstrated how mature and well-formed their responses are in relation to behaviour and attitudes.  They want to be responsible, kind citizens in their approach to others and truly demonstrate their desire to live the federation’s vision to “Do everything in love.”  This is consistently observed to be well modelled to the children by the staff team.
Discussion with staff
A discussion took place with an Early Creer  Teacher.  She feels that there are strong support structures in place to support staff in the management of pupil behaviour and attitudes.  The strategies provided are especially helpful in relation to the management of repeated behaviours.  Policies relating to  behaviour management were carefully covered as part of the school’s induction programme.  Staff are aware that there is consistency in the expectations of behaviour across the school and the school has consequences as part of this process that work well for the children.  The teachers have had training in behaviour management and there has been some additional helpful training relating to the management of more complex behaviours of children with SEND, provided by the SENCo.   New staff are aware that there are staff at the school with a high level of experience and expertise and discussion with colleagues is valued because it enables staff new to the profession to learn from their expertise.  The teacher was able to provide good examples of how support has been provided to enable an improvement in behaviour for a child with specific needs.  There is a good understanding of the school’s expectations of managing classroom routines and maintaining a positive learning environment.  High expectations, clear classroom routines and structure for part of this.  The system for escalating behaviour concerns works well at the school from the ECT’s perspective.  There is effective mentorship and collaboration at the school.  Good advice and helpful strategies are provided by mentors and though discussions with the broader staff team and thy er group partner.  Helpful reflection and feedback is provided by leaders regarding the management of behaviours.  There is a clear understanding of the importance of early intervention in the management of behaviours.  The perspective of the ECT is that staff are very well supported at this school and their professional expertise and methods of feedback is highly respected.
Learning walk
A walk around the school demonstrated engaged, confident learners with excellent behaviour for learning and positive attitudes to learning.  We observed children cooperatively engaging in discussions using talk partners well.
Collective worship is a high priority in the school and this opportunity is used to model exemplary behaviour and enable children to reflect on how they will show such positive and engaging behaviours in their interactions with others.
The use of the Zones of Regulation enables children to articulate their emotions, reflect on their actions and to understand that saying sorry means that actions need to change.
Classrooms have a reflection area.  In one classroom we observe the Storyteller Bible and reflections on The Way of the Cross.
Visual daily timetables can be observed around the school.  This enables children to know what is coming next and to minimise anxieties around points of transition.
Ground rules are clearly displayed around the school, for example in relation to expectations during the PSHE lessons.  In addition the Golden Rules with actions are displayed.
Children have many opportunities to serve the local community.  These opportunities align with the British and school values.  Children are taught to respect peace, try their best, be kind, polite and helpful, look after the school environment, be gentle and honest, to listen to others, to respect individual liberty and to always seek truth and justice.  The are taught to “Do everything in love.”
Children are given movement breaks and the opportunity to participate in sensory circuits is a strategy used for some children.  These activities are designed to support each child’s sensory diet which then regulates behaviour and emotions and puts children in a position to concentrate and learn effectively.  Elements of mindfulness are also used to ground children, bring a sense of calm and enable focus on the learning.
Great care is taken to support the social, emotional and academic development of each individual child.  This started with the youngest children in the EYFS provision.  The transition level of need toolkit is used as a diagnostic tool to identify the presenting needs of individual children into the school.  This then enables the practitioner to ensure provision is put in place to support the specific needs flagged up through this assessment.  There is a clear commitment in this school to addressing needs as the earliest opportunity to ensure that this does not become a more significant challenge as children progress their education. Early intervention is key and this school acts with purpose to address such matters.  Staff work with great commitment to engage parents in the leaning process for their child as they are aware that parents are the most important and influential educators of their children.  What this means is that there is an in-depth knowledge of every child’s individual strengths and barriers to learning.
With the very youngest children high quality provision is observed.  Children learn how to become resilient learners quickly.  They learn social skills to support happy interactions.  They become independent quickly and demonstrate they are able to work cooperatively.  These strengths are attributable to the skill of the practitioners in EYFS.  Children here quickly develop their sense of belonging.  They understand that all families are unique and special and they learn from diversity models.  They celebrate difference and this builds self esteem effectively.  Children are supported to explain their feelings and this helps them to self-regulate quickly.  The learning environment in EYFS is an enabling one that provides high quality secure foundations for the next steps in learning for every child.
The school engages well with the feeder junior and secondary school.  Role models from these schools come in to share their skills with the children.  This happens through the work of the sports leaders, learning ambassadors and kindness ambassadors.  This is excellent practice and supports children’s social skills and learning development.

Discussion with leaders
The school’s uses a pro-social approach to managing behaviour using the STEPS therapeutic thinking framework.  This is outlined in the behaviour policy and ladder.  There is a clear and consistent approach.  Staff have a reflective approach to what the exhibiting behaviour is telling them and understand that all behaviour is communication of a need.  Children are clear about the rules. This was evident through discussion and observation.  The rules are recapped every week in Collective Worship.  Positive behaviours are taught.  There is follow through with behaviour ladder which shows the consequences for specific actions.  There is consistency across school which is regularly updated and communicated to staff.  For example there are published agreed consistencies for teaching assistants which require input from all staff enabling high quality engagement in the learning process for all children  The school has individual plans to support specific children’s learning and behavioural needs.  The school takes active steps to promote equity for all children.  De-escalation strategies use scripts adhering to simple repetitive instructions.  Social stories for children are prepared and some of these were shared relating to the theme of play fighting, adult interaction and making friends.  This helps the children to understand the choices made and how this can be done differently.  It is a useful tool to help pupils to manage behavioural incidents over time.  
Training with teaching assistants takes place to ensure consistency in the implementation of the behaviour policy.  This involves discussion around consequences using the therapeutic approach. The point of this is to ensure that the consequence is prompt and has restorative consequences.  In addition, the school subscribes to the programme “When the adults change, everything changes.”  This enables staff to understand the importance of modelling and consistency in their approach to the management of pupil behaviour.  They are encouraged to reflect upon what the behaviour is telling them and which strategies would be best deployed to support the situation.  Training is also included as part of the induction process for new staff to ensure alignment in approach.
The result of this approach is that staff take ownership of presenting behaviours and use a graduated response to address emerging inconsistencies.  This has empowered the staff team.  Senior leaders are involved more serious, repetitive or one-off dangerous incidents.  The Behaviour ladder is there for staff to respond in an appropriate manner. In addition parents have been educated that the class teacher should always be the first point of contact.
A helpful development to enhance the culture of  positive behaviour management observed at the school is the school’s engagement with “Compassionomics.”  This is teaching the school community about the impact of empathy on enabling everyone to feel they belong.   Advocates in the school provide exceptional support to children with social and emotional needs.

Record keeping
The school uses CPOMS to record incidents, communications and actions relating to all pupils.  This then enables commentaries and actions to be tracked and evaluated.  The approach is tenacious to ensure needs are identified and supported for individual children.  An example of a case study was shared and this clearly demonstrates adherence to the statutory requirements in paragraph  66 of “Keeping Children Safe in Education 2024”  which requires records to include:
“A clear and comprehensive summary of the concern
Details of how the concern was followed up and resolved
A note of action taken, decisions reached and the outcome.”

Endnote
The quality of evidence relating to behaviour and attitudes shared and observed at Victoria CE Infant and Nursery School is exemplary and exceeds the criteria for Good as outlined in the current education inspection framework (EIF).  They key to this success is rigour and tenacity in the implementation of the behaviour policy and proactivity, curiosity and active intervention relating to attendance.  Staff are well supported and trained.  The provision is well supported by exemplary provision in personal development (evaluated on an earlier visit this academic year.)
The key for the school is to ensure confidence in the articulation of what is exceptional about the provision.  It is also about keeping an eye on the proposed changes to the EIF as outlined in the OFSTED Toolkit and ensuring the summary of self-evaluation aligns to the new descriptors (if the school ends up being inspected from September 2025, as is likely, but not definite.)

Recommendations:
To further build upon the high quality provision in behaviour and attitudes observed at the school leaders plan to:
Introduce “Storm break” as a programme to enable consistent physical movement breaks.  Leaders plan to complete the training and effective implementation of this strategy. This is designed to support children's mental health and emotional wellbeing through movement.

Further develop and implement the agreed behavioural consistencies outlined in “The Castle Way” relating to
Meet and greet and transition
Being learning ready
Lesson structure
Using a common language

Use the OFSTED Toolkit as the guide for evaluating provision in Behaviour and Attitudes to ensure this is aligned to the revied inspection framework with effect from September 2025.  The following dates provide HFL training opportunities to learn more about this:
Using the Ofsted toolkits to prepare for your next inspection​ (25/11/25; 29/1/26; 18/3/26; 29/4/26; 23/6/26 – choose one date.)

Date and purpose of next visit: Thursday 26th June pm Summer Visit
                                                         Focus to be confirmed

Name: | Linda A Hardman | District School Effectiveness Adviser for Dacorum
